Sometimes one hears a peep from them
A pleasant surprise awaited Arab Knesset members a week ago Wednesday during another one of those vociferous altercations that erupt regularly with right-wing MKs.
By Aryeh Dayan	Jan. 3, 2002 | 12:00 AM

A pleasant surprise awaited Arab Knesset members a week ago Wednesday during another one of those vociferous altercations that erupt regularly with right-wing MKs.

The start of the incident was routine. Minister Without Portfolio Danny Naveh (Likud), as is his wont, included a provocative sentence in his speech aimed at the Arab MKs. During a speech on Israel's moves to prevent Arafat from visiting Bethlehem for Christmas, he claimed that the Tanzim and Palestinian police routinely sexually harass Christian girls in Beit Jala.

Arab MKs, as usual, did not let Naveh's provocation pass without comment and proceeded to respond obstreperously. "Naveh's a Nazi" and "Danny Goebbels" were just two of the catcalls they hurled.

MK Michael Kleiner (Herut-National Movement) and other right-wing parliamentarians joined in the ruckus which, for the umpteenth time, almost deteriorated into a free-for-all tussle.

The end of the incident was also familiar: Four Knesset members - Ahmed Tibi (Arab Movement for Renewal) , Talab A-Sana (United Arab List), Mohammed Barakeh (Hadash) and Muhammed Kanan (Arab National Party) - were ejected from the hall by the chairman of the session.

What made the incident different and surprised the Arab MKs was that they were joined by three Jewish MKs - Ophir Pines-Paz (Labor), Zahava Gal-On (Meretz) and Joseph Paritzky (Shinui) - who left the hall in protest against Naveh's statement. Pines-Paz even filed a complaint against Naveh in the House Ethics Committee.

The next day, Pines-Paz decided to convene the Knesset Law, Constitution and Justice Committee, which he chairs, to discuss four proposals from Arab MKs concerning equality for the Arab population. Pines-Paz explained that the discussion was intended as a response "to the organized and planned attack and incitement" which he says extreme right-wing parliamentarians are waging against Arab MKs. At the same time, he agrees that some of the language used by Arab MKs "riles unnecessarily."

Most Arab MKs welcomed Pines-Paz's response but also expressed regret that it did not reflect the behavior of most Labor and Meretz MKs. Some even went so far as to say that leftist MKs who should show solidarity with Arab MKs instead "sacrifice" their Arab colleagues to the right. "Their response to the attacks on us are always too limp," said A-Sana. "Once in a while I hear a peep out of them but I've never heard them take an unequivocal stance in condemning the racist attacks on us. They act wishy-washy. Neither here nor there. They don't identify with us, and they don't defend us."

"The sharp response of Pines and Paritzky was heartwarming, precisely because it was unusual," added Tibi. Tibi contends that that MKs from the Zionist left have become alienated from Arab MKs, caving in to Jewish public opinion which has become more extreme. "Their motto is `An eye looks to consensus.' [instead of the line from Hatikva, `an eye looks to Zion']", he said.

This alienation is not only limited to the Knesset, he argues. "When an IDF soldier at the Qalandiyah roadblock shoots warning shots above my head and the head of MK Barakeh, they didn't say a word. A soldier fires at representatives of the public, and not one of them is shocked. When police beat me and MK Issam Makhoul [Hadash] at a demonstration against the Trans-Israel Highway and both of us needed medical treatment, their reaction was even more disheartening. Minister Ephraim Sneh, a member of the Labor Party, reacted exactly the same way as Deputy Public Security Minister Gideon Ezra [Likud]. Both of them argued that we were to blame for the beatings we got. The Zionist left apparently thinks that only Jewish parliamentarians are allowed to demonstrate."

Barakeh, head of the Hadash faction, is convinced that Meretz and Labor MKs take their cues from sentiments among doves. "In private discussion, they tell me that their behavior stems from party considerations. I explain to them that any such party consideration is dwarfed by the price they will pay for this. If the Arab population in Israel feels that no one is the Israeli peace camp is willing to lend an attentive ear, it is likely to get discouraged with politics. That's dangerous. Yisrael Katz [Likud] wants Israeli Arabs to get out of politics and create apartheid. The Jewish peace camp must understand that without Arabs in politics, the right will be victorious on every front."

Deteriorating discourse

MKs Ran Cohen and Anat Maor of Meretz are not impressed by such arguments. "I accept the argument that the right is conducting a racist attack against them," says Maor. "But I also think that they have to understand that there are things that we cannot accept. When an Arab MK calls a Jewish MK `Goebbels' or `Nazi,' he erodes the level of political discourse and uses connotations that must remain outside such a discourse. When a Knesset member - and it is irrelevant if it a Jewish one or an Arab one - sits next to a leader of Hezbollah, he hurts the peace camp and plays into the hands of the right."

"The argument that we keep our distance from Arab MKs in order to satisfy Jewish public opinion is simply untrue," contends Cohen. "We don't distance ourselves from the Arab public at all, but only from its representatives, who even before October 2000 began making all sorts of extreme nationalist declarations that are unacceptable. The call of MK Abdulmalik Dehamshe to break the hands and legs of police was heard before the murder of Arab demonstrators in the events of October."

He opines that Jewish-Arab cooperation, which has been painstakingly built since the 1950s, has crumbled due to several processes within the Arab leadership. "They identify completely with all of Arafat's illusions, and some of them identify with the ideology of Hamas. Every week they are ejected from the plenary, but I have never seen them even once going berserk on behalf of the disabled, the unemployed or residents of the Negev."

Such statements make A-Sana's blood boil. "I'm not willing to tolerate this patronizing attitude any longer," he says. "It is unconscionable that MKs from Meretz continue to think that they know better than we do what is good for us or that they try to dictate the stand we should take. They must understand that we want a partnership, but we are not willing to be collaborators. There's a big difference between a partner and a collaborator."

The deep rift highlights the growing gap between the Zionist left, where anti-Arab sentiment even among Jewish doves has intensified, and Hadash and Arab parties, which reflect intensifying anti-Zionist and pro-Palestinian sentiment among the Arab population.

The rift has grown so big that it has almost been forgotten that just two-and-a-half years ago, in the summer of 1999, these parties joined Meretz and Labor in supporting Barak's bid to become prime minister.

Barak's behavior after the election helped bring about the rift. His refusal to invite Hadash and the Arab parties for coalition talks left scars on the Arab parties and their public, which gave Barak more than 90 percent of their votes. The weakening of Hadash, which has an Arab-Jewish character, and the strengthening of Balad and the Islamic Movement, which have an agenda different from that of the Israeli peace camp, deepened the rift. The burgeoning of parties in the Arab sector (represented by 10 MKs from four factions and seven parties) increases competition among Arab MKs for media attention and causes them to express themselves in ever more extreme terms.

The collapse of the Oslo process has only added to the rift. The accumulative effect of all these processes is highlighted in proceedings of the Knesset plenary. The conflicts between the right and Arab MKs become ever sharper, and most MKs from the Zionist left choose to watch from the sidelines.

Not a sacrificial lamb

Barakeh accuses his leftist Jewish colleagues of losing their political and moral backbone. "The mistake of Meretz and Labor is that instead of thinking strategically, they take the easy way out politically. They must understand that the process of delegitimization will not stop with Arabs. Yossi Beilin and Yossi Sarid are already termed these days as traitors. Zvi Hendel [Likud] has even put Shimon Peres on the list of traitors. The left must learn from the lessons of history. It starts with the minority and spreads to the majority."

"The strategy of the right is absolutely clear," adds A-Sana. "They are delegitimizing us because they know that the left will never be able to get back into power without the Arabs. From the moment that the right manages to make us illegitimate and pulls us out of the Knesset as Yisrael Katz has proposed, it will have succeeded in neutralizing the influence of the left and will gain control of the state. It is a smart strategy and the left is responding to it in a stupid manner."

Some Arab MKs, such as veteran politician Hashem Mahameed [United Arab List], don't limit themselves to criticizing only the Jewish left but also reserve ammunition for their own camp. Mahameed, unlike some of the other Arab MKs, says he doesn't feel like the "sacrificial lamb" of the Zionist left. He distances himself from the style of some of his Arab colleagues and contends that part of his role is to "clarify to the Jewish public, which is the majority in this country, that the struggle we are waging is not against Jews or against the state but rather against the establishment and the government."

Most Arab MKs, Mahameed is convinced, fail in this task. "When I heap all the blame on the Jews, when I fail to recognize the state and when I declare that the struggle is between two peoples, between Jews and Arabs, I serve Kleiner, Landau and Danny Naveh.

"The role of an MK," he adds in a hint at MK Azmi Bishara, "is to lead the public without distancing oneself too much from it. A person who dispenses slogans on autonomy for Israeli Arabs, is doing something irresponsible. It's a dangerous idea. All of us are aware of the danger of transfer. I don't want us to wave around radical slogans today and cry tomorrow over the results."


Home Print Edition Opinion
Jerusalem should take a look at Belfast
It's hard to believe, but someone returning nowadays from Belfast carries some baggage full of optimism and hope that perhaps all is not lost in Jerusalem.
By Meron Benvenisti	| Jan. 10, 2002 | 12:00 AM

It's hard to believe, but someone returning nowadays from Belfast carries some baggage full of optimism and hope that perhaps all is not lost in Jerusalem. Those who remember the warnings of the 1970s and early 1980s that Jerusalem could yet turn into another Belfast - and the intercommunal violence deteriorate into a conflict like Northern Ireland's - can now say to themselves: If only Jerusalem could be like contemporary Belfast.

Things have changed so much that the comparison itself seems farfetched: How can one compare communities that have decided after generations of violent conflict to give up the violence and channel the conflict between them onto the political plane, with communities whose relationship is based on violence, coercion, delegitimizing the other and mutual terrorism?

Or maybe the two cities are on two different places along a historical time line leading from conflict to conciliation. Belfast is already a generation ahead of Jerusalem, situated in that positive realm between political arrangements and conciliation, while Jerusalem is stuck far behind, sinking ever deeper into the abyss of alienation and brutality.

A Jerusalemite visiting Belfast immediately notices the similarities between his ancient city and the very different Irish-British city.

In both places, the world is divided into two: "Us" and "them," "theirs" and "ours." Both are polarized cities, in which tribalism interprets itself from the interpersonal into political structures, and in particular into territorial homogeneity, painting their neighborhoods with national colors: orange and green in Belfast, blue and green in Jerusalem.

The seams between the neighborhoods are more dramatic in Belfast than in Jerusalem. No fewer than 25 dividing lines, built of brick and iron walls, divide the Protestant and Catholic communities, with the lines emphasized by huge wall murals making tangible the national identities of the residents.

These lines of division, euphemistically called "peace lines," are the focal point of serious violence nowadays, as well. The number of barriers between the armed Protestant and Catholic communities not only didn't decrease since 1994, but indeed grew. In Belfast, one learns the difference between political arrangements and genuine intercommunal reconciliation. Since the start of the Good Friday process that gave birth to the local joint government, the segregation between Protestants and Catholics has grown, only now it's called "benign apartheid."

The reduction of the conflict between the armed militias did not eliminate all the violence, but changed its nature: now it's aimed inward, to punish "deviants," or criminal elements, as well as the violence of youths and warring drug gangs. That teaches us that eliminating the external threat brings the internal differences into focus, and "peace" is not a utopian state.

Many in the Protestant majority are furious about what they perceive as "overly generous concessions" by their leaders and their government, who sold the other side their privileges, sacrificed political interests and sacred symbols. That, too, is the price paid for "painful concessions," and it endangers the stability of the process.

So, if that's the situation in Belfast, why the optimism? The optimism is derived from the way the political, academic and social elite manages the public affairs and informs the public discourse. A broad network of government, semi-governmental and voluntary agencies successfully runs what they call "a divided society in transition."

Organizations from both sides are partners in this endeavor and characteristic of their activities is the way they avoid any activity that is not aimed at building bridges, and forbid emotionally-loaded terminology.

For example, you won't hear the term "terror" or "terrorist"; after all, "a terrorist with blood on his hands" is now education minister in the province.

So, if it is possible in Belfast, maybe all is not lost in Jerusalem. A visit to Northern Ireland is warmly recommended to the mayor of Jerusalem and the Public Security Minister.


Home Print Edition News
South Africa's Tutu says Mugabe has gone `bonkers'
By Reuters	| Jan. 13, 2002 | 12:00 AM

JOHANNESBURG - Veteran anti-apartheid campaigner Archbishop Desmond Tutu said yesterday Zimbabwe's President Robert Mugabe had gone "bonkers in a big way" for disregarding the rule of law and assuming greater powers.

In an interview with the Saturday Star, Tutu joined mounting international criticism over Mugabe's leadership and said the sensible thing for the Zimbabwean leader would be to step down.

"Mugabe seems to have gone bonkers in a big way. It is very dangerous when you subvert the rule of law in your country, when you don't even respect the judgments of your judges...then you are on the slippery slope of perdition," Tutu said.

"It is a great sadness what has happened to President Mugabe. He was one of Africa's best leaders, a bright spark, a debonair, well-spoken and well-read person," the 1984 Nobel Peace Prize Winner told the newspaper.

Zimbabwe's parliament, where Mugabe's ZANU-PF has a comfortable majority, passed two controversial laws on Thursday - one that criminalized criticism of Mugabe and gave sweeping security powers to the government and another banning independent election monitors for the March 9-10 presidential polls and denying voting rights to millions of Zimbabweans abroad.

Faced with growing international isolation and the threat of sanctions, Zimbabwe said on Friday it would accept international observers for the elections, but strictly on its own terms.

The European Union is under mounting pressure to impose economic sanctions on Zimbabwe and suspend development aid. There are also calls for Zimbabwe to be suspended from the 54-nation Commonwealth.

South Africa broke a two-day silence on developments in its northern neighbor on Friday, saying it was "unacceptable" for the Zimbabwean army to signal it would only accept a victory by Mugabe, 77, in the election.

South Africa's President Thabo Mbeki said in a separate comment that "wrong things are happening in our neighborhood."

Tutu said Mbeki's government needed to take a stronger stance and its policy of quiet diplomacy had failed.

Mugabe, in power since 1980 when the former Rhodesia gained independence from Britain, faces a serious election challenge from Morgan Tsvangirai, leader of the main opposition Movement for Democratic Change.


Home News
Truth commission set for traumatized East Timor
By Reuters	| Jan. 16, 2002 | 12:00 AM

JAKARTA - East Timor will formally open a truth and reconciliation commission next week in a major step toward shedding light on widespread human rights abuses committed during Indonesia's brutal rule over the territory.

Similar in principle to the South African Truth and Reconciliation Commission that probed apartheid-era crimes, officials said the East Timor body was vital to helping Timorese traumatized by the past deal with their suffering.

Officials said seven Timorese national commissioners were scheduled to be sworn in on Monday to mark the official opening of the body, although hearings would not start immediately.

"Many Timorese want answers from those who caused their loss and suffering," Xanana Gusmao, East Timor's independence hero and the man expected to become the territory's first president, said in remarks carried in commission documents obtained by Reuters.

"With answers, people can start the healing process and close the horrible chapter in their lives."

The establishment of the Commission for Reception, Truth and Reconciliation in East Timor comes as Jakarta moves closer to putting 19 suspects, including three generals, on trial over alleged major human rights abuses in East Timor linked to the territory's vote for independence in 1999.

The vote prompted pro-Jakarta militias, with support from the Indonesian army, to go on a rampage in which the UN estimates more than 1,000 people were killed.

East Timor, currently administered by the United Nations, will become formally independent on May 20.

Indonesia's 1975 invasion and subsequent 24-year occupation of the former Portuguese territory left more than 200,000 people -- a quarter of the population -- dead from fighting, famine and disease. The United Nations never recognized Jakarta's rule.

The commission, being set up by the UN administration with widespread support from Timorese political leaders, will operate for two years. It can extend for a further six months if needed.


Home Print Edition News
PM okays plan to `envelop Jerusalem'
Prime Minister Ariel Sharon yesterday approved a heightened security plan to "envelop Jerusalem" and said it should also include certain Jewish West Bank communities and Palestinian communities in Area B.
By Haaretz Staff	| Jan. 30, 2002 | 12:00 AM

Prime Minister Ariel Sharon yesterday approved a heightened security plan to "envelop Jerusalem" and said it should also include Jewish West Bank communities such as Ma'aleh Adumim, Givat Ze'ev, and Har Gilo which are outside the capital's jurisdiction, and Palestinian communities in Area B.

Sharon emphasized that Jerusalem would be treated as a "single whole" that "includes Jewish and Arab neighborhoods as one." Among the Palestinian communities to be included are Bir Naballah, Biddya, Abu Dis and Azzariyeh. Sharon noted that the political status of Area B Palestinian communities included in the plan would not change. Area B communities are supposed to be under Palestinian Authority civil control and Israeli military control.

Sharon discussed the plan yesterday with National Security Adviser Uzi Dayan and afterward said he considered it to be "an essential element in securing Jerusalem." He ordered the National Security Council and the Ministry of Public Security to make the necessary changes to expand the area covered by the plan so that it can be presented soon to the security cabinet for budget approval.

The plan, drafted jointly by Dayan and Jerusalem Police Chief Mickey Levy, calls for the construction of an 11-kilometer wall along the seam line between East and West Jerusalem; the establishment of roadblocks between the eastern and western parts of the city; the installation of video cameras along the seam line; the creation of five Border Police units to patrol the seam line and the introduction of new identification technologies such as thermal sensors and night-vision equipment.

Expanding the plan beyond the borders of Jerusalem would mean some of the checkpoints currently around the city will be moved further away. But government sources said the inclusion of Jewish communities outside of Jerusalem had political significance and was not only a security consideration. It signaled a revival of the concept of a "greater Jerusalem," they noted.

Though parts of the plan may be adopted, it seems unlikely to be approved in full. Sharon, as well as Public Security Minister Uzi Landau and Jerusalem Mayor Ehud Olmert all object to walls or fences that could be interpreted as a repartitioning of the city. When Landau described the plan to the Knesset Foreign Affairs and Defense Committee on Monday, he insisted it would not include any fences within the capital. It would merely channel people entering the city from outside through a number of well-guarded routes, he said.

Police sources yesterday expressed skepticism that the government would agree to fund the plan. "These plans come up for discussion after every wave of attacks, but in practice nothing happens," said one senior police officer.

Palestinian officials, reacting to reports of the plan, said it would never succeed in protecting Israelis.

"These oppressive Israeli military measures in East Jerusalem will not give Israelis security," said Ziyad Abu Zayad, who holds the Jerusalem Affairs portfolio in the Palestinian Authority. He said Arab East Jerusalem communities had already been cut off from each other due to military trenches and checkposts. "Those measures did not give Israelis security and neither will the new plan. The problem is not a security one but a political one and therefore it cannot be solved by military oppression," he said.

Yasser Abed Rabbo, minister of information and culture in the PA, said the plan constituted "heightened provocation" by the Sharon government. He said it showed Sharon was not interested in a political situation and wanted only to "create bantustans," a reference to the semi-autonomous Black regions created by apartheid South Africa. Both Abed Rabbo and Abu Zayad said the PA was working through international diplomatic contacts to sabotage the "enveloping Jerusalem" plan.


Home Print Edition News
News in Brief
Feb. 3, 2002 | 12:00 AM


Doctor says Arafat's checkup overdue

Yasser Arafat's routine checkup is overdue and it is "inhuman" of Israel to keep him from medical attention, a leading Jordanian neurologist who regularly examines the Palestinian leader said yesterday. "Mr. Arafat must undergo medical checkups regularly, but he can't do that because Israel is locking him up and tanks are parked outside the walls of his headquarters," said Dr. Ashraf al-Kurdi. "It is inhuman to lock up a man who needs medical attention." He declined to discuss Arafat's health. (AP)

Toddler `sleepwalks' streets before dawn

A 40-year-old Ashdod mother is being questioned after her 2-and-a-half year old son was found by residents at 4 A.M. yesterday, half-naked and shivering from cold, and wandering a local street. Police managed to find the mother when an officer remembered the same boy being found in similar circumstances six months ago. The single mother told police the child sleepwalks. Social welfare authorities have taken the boy into care. The woman may face charges of criminal negligence. (Aliza Arbeli)

Orient House NGO comes under scrutiny

The Institute for Arab Studies, based in Orient House in East Jerusalem, has violated its charter as a research center and promotes the interests of the Palestinian Authority in Jerusalem, according to a report Amiram Bogat, the registrar of NGOs (non-government organizations) sent to Interior Minister Eli Yishai. He will send the report to Minister of Public Security Uzi Landau today and will recommend opening a criminal investigation into the institute. (Mazal Mualem)

Israeli held in Thai teen sex investigation

Police in Thailand have arrested a 62-year-old Israeli suspecting of having sex with male Cambodian teenagers, aged 15-17. He was arrested in a town 90 minutes' drive from Bangkok. Amnon Kalmer, the Bangkok consul, met with the suspect who told him he has been living in Thailand and Cambodia and is innocent. "I gave one of the teenagers keys to stay in my room there while I traveled to Cambodia. I am innocent," he told the consul. A local judge will soon decide whether to prosecute the suspect. (Itim)

Undercover snoops check airline security

Israel sends "undercover" passengers on foreign flights that land at Ben-Gurion International Airport to examine their flight security procedures. As a result of lapses found by these undercover passengers, Transport Minister Ephraim Sneh has ordered airport authorities to start banning flights by companies whose security procedures do not meet required standards. The companies in question are mostly from the CIS and Eastern Europe. The undercover experts observed security flaws on their flights, such as the lack of a secure door separating the pilots from passengers. (Zohar Blumenkrantz)

NII workers vow to step up strike actions

Workers' committees from the National Insurance Institute meet today and are expected to escalate work disputes, which have been going on for the past two-and-a-half weeks at all NII branches. Among other measures, the workers might refuse to handle entitlements paid to victims of terror. Another possible new measure could be a refusal to handle new entitlement requests that have been sent in by mail. NII workers are demanding that 150 to 200 new employees be added to branches around the country, and also that security measures be beefed up, in light of violent incidents involving unhappy entitlement seekers at NII branches. (Haim Bior)

Jazeera squabbles with CNN over tape

DOHA - Al-Jazeera TV yesterday accused CNN of getting a copy of its October interview with Osama bin Laden in "an unknown way," of broadcasting excerpts without prior permission, and then questioning Jazeera's integrity for not airing it. "Jazeera sees a need to discuss this issue in the framework of the cooperation agreement," Jazeera said yesterday. "Jazeera does not accept any doubt on its credibility from any source." Jazeera, which had a cooperation arrangement with CNN, earlier said it was severing its relations with CNN and would "punish the individuals who stole this video and distributed it illegally." (Reuters)

Court evicts Winnie from Mandela home

JOHANNESURG - A Johannesburg court ordered Winnie Madikizela-Mandela to vacate the home she once shared with former President Nelson Mandela. Mandela bought the house in 1990, shortly after he was freed from prison, where the South African apartheid state jailed him for 27 years. He divorced Madikizela-Mandela in 1996, and bequeathed the house to the private Soweto Heritage Foundation the following year. Madikizela-Mandela, a ruling party lawmaker, asked a court to set the transaction aside. In the meantime, she turned the four-room house into a museum, charging a $1.30 entrance fee. (AP)

Kuwaiti oil minister to resign over oil field fire

KUWAIT - Kuwait's oil minister said yesterday he would resign to take responsibility for a deadly oil field fire that has temporarily halted production in four northern fields. Thursday's fire at Rawdatain, Kuwait's second-largest oil field, was sparked by an oil leak at a gathering center. Four workers died and 19 were injured. Oil Minister Adel al-Subaih said he had written and signed his resignation and would submit it yesterday or today. (AP)

Zimbabwe backtracks on media control

HARARE - Zimbabwe yesterday backtracked on its sweeping media control laws, indicating the measures will not be imposed immediately and may not be enforced at all, a state-run newspaper reported. The state's Herald newspaper, a mouthpiece for official policy, said it learned from the office of the Attorney General, the government's chief law officer, the bill "may take quite some time before it becomes law, if at all." The laws, widely condemned by the international community and journalists, still have to be signed into law by President Robert Mugabe. (AP)

India, Pakistan trade fire, seven die in Kashmir

JAMMU - Indian and Pakistani forces exchanged mortar and heavy machine-gun fire across the cease-fire line in disputed Kashmir yesterday as seven people died in separate incidents in the troubled region, according to a senior Indian police official. The official described the firing as "routine and below average." Police said seven people including six militants had been killed in different incidents in Jammu and Kashmir state since Friday evening. (Reuters)

Jail for Malaysian allegedly tied to Sept. 11

KUALA LUMPUR - A former army captain who allegedly hosted two September 11 hijackers and other suspected al-Qaida members during their stay in Malaysia in January 2000 has been sent to a prison camp without trial for two years, a government official in Kuala Lumpur said yesterday. Yazid Sufaat, 37, was detained along with 22 others for interrogation in an anti-terrorism crackdown launched last month under the Internal Security Act, which provides for indefinite detention without trial. Police said the suspects belonged to a group that was trained by al-Qaida in Afghanistan and had links with Islamic groups in Indonesia, the Philippines and Singapore. (AP)


Home Print Edition Features
Waiting for the peace marchers
According to a well-placed military source, just before Tanzim commander Raed Karmi was killed, Yasser Arafat was closer than ever to a decision to order the armed intifada to switch to nonviolent civil disobedience.
By Akiva Eldar	| Feb. 5, 2002 | 12:00 AM

According to a well-placed military source, just before Tanzim commander Raed Karmi was killed, Yasser Arafat was closer than ever to a decision to order the armed intifada to switch to nonviolent civil disobedience. The terrorist attacks and the shootings that followed Karmi's "work accident," as Defense Minister Benjamin Ben-Eliezer referred to his assassination, postponed the change - but didn't cancel it. At the Palestinian Authority offices in Ramallah, officials are studying the way South African blacks challenged apartheid. Arafat has lately been speaking of a march on Jerusalem. The flood of articles written by Palestinian leaders for the Israeli and American press are an indication of the desire to turn the swords into plowshares.

Israel's security services have also heard about the attempt to organize nonviolent demonstrations. At various levels of the army, they are trying to figure out how to cope with a scenario in which thousands of unarmed Palestinians march from Ramallah, Jericho and Bethlehem, toward the army checkpoints with which the IDF has surrounded Jerusalem.

One question bothering the field commanders, for example, is what should a platoon commander do if his soldiers are faced with hundreds of women and Palestinian school children carrying only peace placards, walking toward the settlement of Psagot? What if dozens of marches leave at the same time from all over the West Bank, heading toward the settlements that surround them? The answers coming up in the internal discussions are that tanks and helicopters are useless against demonstrators armed with peace posters.

The IDF is also preparing for an opposite scenario: how to deal with the PA's own security forces if they launch a coordinated assault on the settlements. What would the IDF do if Jibril Rajoub and Mohammed Dahlan, who have begun coordinating their activities, decide they have nothing left to lose and officially turn the PA into the "enemy"? The answer: tanks and choppers will demolish the police headquarters and reconquer the Palestinian cities. But the tanks and helicopters won't be very useful running occupied Palestinian cities. It turns out that the civil administration has not tried to run hospitals and schools in Ramallah for a long time.

The most fashionable expressions nowadays in army slang are "civilian leverage" and "the price of defeat." During military exercises, one can hear a commander urging on his officers with "give them some civilian leverage," or "make them feel the price of defeat." Translation: Cut off the electricity to a neighborhood in Ramallah, or bomb the Palestinian TV station.

Along with "security for the residents of Binyamin" - meaning settlers - which leaves no room for doubt about what the IDF is fighting for, the "strategy" coming down the chain of command from Chief of Staff Shaul Mofaz to the unit operations officers is uniform and unified: "The price of defeat the Palestinians have to pay must be as high as possible, to guarantee that our situation in the negotiations after we win the war will be better than it was beforehand." That's the same Mofaz who charged the opponents of military service in the territories with hidden political motives.

Ever the tactician

After he heard about Prime Minister Ariel Sharon's meeting with the three Palestinian leaders, another Palestinian official, who was not invited to the prime minister's residence, asked an Israeli acquaintance to explain why the premier needed the meeting - and why he then hurried to tell everyone about it as soon as it was over, getting himself into trouble with Benny Elon.

"Don't tell me Sharon needs to please the Americans before he goes to Washington," said the Palestinian. "We all know that there's no American pressure on him to change his policies."

The Palestinians are convinced that the American-Israeli game is fixed. But, presumably along with the reports of the warm greeting he can expect in Washington, Sharon also has read about the pressure the Europeans, Egyptians and Saudi Arabians are applying on the Americans. The EU's Javier Solana, for example, is telling the Bush administration that the Mitchell report, which Solana helped write, has exhausted its usefulness.

The meeting with Arafat's envoys was therefore meant to provide Bush and his people with ammunition for repelling the ideas raised by the Europeans and the Arabs - like holding a Palestinian election or referendum. The Europeans suggest that after the voting, the international observers of the ballot boxes could move over to the Palestinian prisons, to make sure the Palestinians do indeed keep the arrested terrorists under lock and key.

Sharon - and Shimon Peres - stubbornly reject the European and Canadian offer to bring the observers in through the back door, by beefing up the small European unit that conducts security coordination in the territories. Its head, Alistaire Crooke, has experience in Ireland and Afghanistan, and is doing a lot behind the scenes to calm things in the territories.

But mostly, the late night visit to the Sharon's official residence was meant to serve Sharon's real purpose: to keep Peres shackled to the government. People in the government and Knesset corridors say Peres has his sad sack look. His associates insist that he's been looking for a way out of the government, which makes no effort to even express readiness for a political settlement with the Palestinians. If Peres was looking for an excuse, the meeting between Sharon and the three Palestinians on Wednesday night, and the leak about it on Friday, has undermined the search completely.

Coalition games

Yossi Beilin, whose political patron was Shimon Peres, has stopped counting on an exodus of his friends from the Labor Party. Last week he and Yuli Tamir went to a meeting where the Peace Coalition decided to start the largest, most expensive peace campaign since the start of the intifada. Yesterday they took off for Germany, to take part in a meeting with Palestinians organized by German Foreign Minister Joschka Fischer, who wants to prove that Arafat and Sharon aren't everything.

Seven organizations are participating in the events that begin the day after tomorrow: Peace Now, Meretz, Labor doves, the Kibbutz Movement, Shomer Hatzair, Netivot Shalom, and Green Line. Hundreds of activists have already begun organizing for a series of demonstrations to take place between now and June. They plan to invade the highway intersections, offering drivers a sheaf of bumper stickers, with slogans like "Leave the territories to return to ourselves," "Leave the territories and clean up the economy," and "Leave the territories and save the state."

At the end of a lengthy debate with Tamir, among others, Beilin finally agreed to include a call for "some unilateral steps" that would include immediate evacuation of the settlements in Gaza and in the heart of the Palestinian population in the West Bank. It will be interesting to see if Knesset Speaker Avraham Burg, one of the new activists in the Peace Coalition, shows up on Thursday for the demonstration aimed against the policies of the coalition in which his party continues to star.


Home Print Edition News
Reserve generals back unilateral withdrawal
After four months of intense discussion, the Council for Peace and Security are to mount a public campaign for a unilateral Israeli withdrawal from all of Gaza and much of the West Bank.
By Lily Galili	| Feb. 18, 2002 | 12:00 AM

After four months of intense discussion, the Council for Peace and Security, a group of 1,000 top-level reserve generals, colonels, and Shin Bet and Mossad officials, are to mount a public campaign for a unilateral Israeli withdrawal from all of Gaza and much of the West Bank.

Taking care to avoid the term "separation" - council member Shlomo Avineri, a former foreign ministry director general, said it smacks of apartheid - the organization is calling for evacuating Gaza, dismantling 50 settlements, the immediate establishment of a Palestinian state, and immediate peace talks with Palestinians, whether there is a cease-fire or not.

The debate inside the organization involved some 300 of its members, and about 80 percent of the full membership has signed on to the campaign. It will include public appearances, bumper stickers, and a pamphlet titled "Saying shalom to the Palestinians." During the debates, many members raised objections to a unilateral withdrawal from the territories. The arguments ranged from how it would forestall talks with the Palestinians, because a withdrawal would be seen as similar to the "escape" from Lebanon, to the opinion that the council should not deal with withdrawal, which implies leaving settlements.

But as the debate continued, a consensus evolved to encompass 80 percent of the membership that believes the immediate establishment and recognition of a Palestinian state would force the Palestinian leadership to change its behavior. Council sources said Palestinians shown the plan are firmly opposed to it because they fear the new lines to be drawn - mostly along the Green Line - would become permanent, if only de facto, borders.

"I went into the discussions without a firm opinion," said reserve major general Danny Rothschild, president of the council. "But I was convinced by the contacts I have through back channels with Palestinians in recent months. I've learned from them that the street has taken over the entire moderate camp, and the moderate positions they take behind closed doors change the minute there's fear that they will be exposed to the threatening street. I also took into account the demographic issue, and without any chance right now for negotiations, it requires withdrawal in order to preserve the Jewish character of the state."

But more than anything else, Rothschild said the deciding factor for him was the sprouting movement of soldiers refusing to serve in the territories, even before the officers letter issued late last month that has already grown to more than 200 signatories. "Four months ago it was clear to me that the movement would grow if we continue calling up reserves to accompany settlers to music lessons and to protect real estate that has nothing to do with ideology."

According to Rothschild, the council members "said to ourselves that precisely because we aren't politicians, but people who look at the situation through a security perspective of how to use power, it was clear that those two jeeps and a tank that accompany a settler who refuses to use a bypass road, would do much more good if they were on the seam," meaning on both sides of the Green Line, the pre-1967 armistice lines. "Shifting a company of soldiers from protecting a settlement to protecting the seam is the proper use of force," says Rothschild.

Unlike some of the other unilateral withdrawal plans, like "Life Fence," for example, the council's plan involves evacuating some 40-50 settlements, where some 15 percent of the settlers live. The council has detailed maps, but it won't make them public yet to avoid being perceived as an alterative to the army.

The council plan will be dubbed "Saying shalom to the Palestinians," using the double meaning of both farewell and peace for the word shalom, and includes a full withdrawal from Gaza, except for a narrow zone along the international border with Egypt; new military deployment along a new line east and south of the Green Line in the northern West Bank, and east and north of it in the south Mt. Hebron area. The Green Line would become the new line in the Bethlehem and Ramallah areas. According to the plan, Israelis would remain - at this stage - in the Jordan Valley, the Gush Etzion bloc, the Ariel finger, and in Kiryat Arba and in the Jewish neighborhood in downtown Hebron.

The plan does not touch on the issue of Jerusalem, except for noting that by moving troops out of other places, more will be available for protecting Jerusalem. "This is not a 100 percent solution," Rothschild admits, "but the plan solves the anomaly of there being the most number of troops in the places with the least number of settlers to protect."

One of the most vehement of the council member opposed to the plan is reserve major general Shlomo Gazit, a member of the council's executive. Gazit argued for redeployment to new lines, but said as much as possible has to be left to negotiations. Indeed, Gazit seems to be expressing the ambivalence in other organizations that back unilateral separation but are afraid it will sabotage any negotiations with the Palestinians.

Thus, the Peace Coalition, comprised of Peace Now, Meretz and Labor Party doves, is speaking in two voices as it calls for unilateral separation and for negotiations. Indeed, the bumper sticker the Council for Peace and Security encompasses that ambivalence: "Withdrawal for security, talks for peace."

In the past, the council threw its considerable weight behind the Oslo agreements, most of whose architects are now opposed to a unilateral withdrawal. But Rothschild has a different view. "The negotiations for a permanent agreement have to be based on Oslo. But an army commander cannot be dogmatic. When conditions change on the ground, he must change his behavior. If Oslo is dead, it's because we killed it, and now we're shooting. But now there's no choice except to do what's best for us."

At the latest session of the council's executive, last week, which was attended by among others reserve major generals Nati Sharoni and Ami Ayalon, the former Shin Bet chief, the executive challenged Prime Minister Ariel Sharon's policy of refusing to negotiate with the Palestinians under fire. The council called for immediate talks, under fire, with the Palestinians, and for the immediate evacuation of isolated settlements that require a large military presence to protect.

Sharon refused to meet with them

During the months of preparation, discussing the plan, council members met with a host of figures, including Foreign Minister Shimon Peres and former justice minister Yossi Beilin, who oppose unilateral withdrawal; Haim Ramon who favors unilateral withdrawal and a permanent agreement with international peacekeeping forces, which the council rejects; Minister Dan Meridor, who supports separation primarily for demographic reasons, and with Defense Minister Benjamin Ben-Eliezer, who opposes evacuating settlements.

As part of their planned public campaign, the council was supposed to meet with the Labor Party Knesset faction this week, to present the plan. But the meeting was canceled at the last minute, with the conventional wisdom saying Ben-Eliezer did not want to provide a platform to the proponents of unilateral withdrawal, like Ramon.

The council is due to meet President Moshe Katsav and former prime minister Benjamin Netanyahu, to present the plan. Prime Minister Ariel Sharon has refused to meet them.

The council has an appointment at Rafael in two weeks time to examine new technologies, including a new security fence, developed by the state-owned weapons R&D firm, that could be integrated into their security plan.


Home News
Arafat rejects PM's separation plan as 'violation of agreements'
By Reuters	and Haaretz Service	| Feb. 23, 2002 | 12:00 AM

Palestinian Authority Chairman Yasser Arafat on Saturday rejected Prime Minister Ariel Sharon's plan to create buffer zones between Israel and Palestinian territories. "This so-called separation plan is a violation of the agreements," Arafat told Reuters in an interview in his office in the West Bank city of Ramallah.

"It creates cantons in our Palestinian areas, and the Oslo (1993 interim peace) agreement prevents cantons Sharon is trying to create. He has no right to do this. It's in violation of international law."

Arafat said Sharon's address "was not a speech of peace." The Palestinian leader said Israel's killing of some 40 Palestinians in the West Bank and Gaza Strip in the past week "was terrorism practiced by the occupation against our people, and a military escalation."

Arafat again called for the implementation of a U.S.-backed truce-to-talks plan and for the deployment of international observers "to end this aggression and this siege imposed on our people and areas." He said the Palestinians had been ready to make contact with Israelis at all levels, and noted senior Palestinian officials had met Sharon on February 1. "Unfortunately, all these meetings ended without results," Arafat said. "I repeated my call for a ceasefire on Thursday and Israel's response was more military escalation."

Arafat said he welcomed an appeal made to him and Sharon in Stockholm on Saturday by leaders of Europe, South American, South Africa, New Zealand and Canada to end the bloodshed. "This is one of the most difficult crises (I've gone through). But I can see it coming -- God willing -- a Palestinian child will raise the Palestinian flag on the walls of Jerusalem, whether they like it or not, and we are pursuing this path," he said.

Gulf media slam 'racist' Israeli buffer zone plan Gulf Arab media blasted Sharon's plan to set up "buffer zones" as a "racist" move modeled after South Africa's defunct apartheid system.

Many commentators in Saturday's newspapers said such a move, which Sharon proposed in a speech late Thursday, would "imprison" both sides and fail to solve the conflict. "In his speech, Sharon surpassed the racist orders that the world witnessed in Rhodesia and South Africa," Saudi Arabia's al-Watan newspaper said in an editorial.

There has been no official reaction by Gulf Arab states to Sharon's speech in favor of "security separation" to protect Israelis from a Palestinian uprising against occupation. But Gulf newspapers usually reflect government stances.

"The zones would only increase Israel's isolation and deepen its nature as a racist oppressive regime, similar to the one the world fought in South Africa," said the United Arab Emirates' Arabic daily al-Ittihad. It said there was also a danger that buffer zones would lead to a future independent Palestinian state that was fragmented.

The Saudi al-Riyadh daily said forming what it called "Israeli ghettos" would widen the rift with the Palestinians. "Sharon's zones can be called adjoining cells for Palestinians and Israelis," it said. "The mind imprisoned in historical complexes cannot envision the future. The mind must progress to respond to the echoing voice of peace."

Sharon's plan to set up buffer zones drew Palestinian outrage Friday. Palestinian Information Minister Yasser Abed Rabbo condemned the move, which followed a day of Israeli reprisal raids that killed eight people, as an attempt to "create jails for the Palestinian people within their cities, towns and villages."

Palestinian cabinet minister Saeb Erekat said Friday that the intentions behind Sharon's plan are to annex 58 percent of the territories to Israel and to give the Palestinians only 42 percent. "No Palestinian will accept this," Erekat said.

Palestinian cabinet minister Nabil Shaath said Thursday that Sharon's address offered "no hope" in ending the nearly 17 months of fighting.

"This speech offered... no complete suggestions, and opened no way to ending the confrontation and heading back to the peace table," he told CNN. "If separation is intended to stop suicidal missions, it has failed. If it's intended to stop trade and communication between the two people, it has succeeded to the misery of both."

Mustafa Barghouti, a Palestinian analyst, described Sharon's address as "an amazing speech of a defeated man." He said Sharon looked "desperate, tired, confused and bankrupt" because all of his policies have failed.

A U.S. State Department official said Friday that Sharon's plan could amount to the kind of unilateral action which Washington has opposed, but said the United States was reserving judgment. However, U.S. Secretary of State Colin Powell said Friday that the United States was concerned about a "serious escalation" in the violence.

"We are concerned. As soon as I get back to Washington, I'll spend a good part of the weekend re-engaging both sides," he told reporters aboard Air Force One returning from a trip to Asia with President George W. Bush.


Home Print Edition Sports
Spearhead Donald hangs up test boots
By Reuters	| Feb. 26, 2002 | 12:00 AM

JOHANNESBURG - Allan Donald, who announced his retirement from test cricket yesterday, spearheaded South Africa's bowling attack for almost his entire career. The 35-year-old played 72 test matches for his country, taking 330 wickets at an average of 22.25, with a career-best haul of eight for 71 against Zimbabwe, in Harare, in 1995-96.

Donald took five wickets in a test innings 20 times, and 10 in a test match three times.

The only South African so far to take 300 test wickets, Donald will be remembered as one of the finest fast bowlers of his generation. He is also, perhaps, the best produced in a country that also gave the game the likes of Trevor Goddard, Mike Procter and Peter Pollock.

Donald was one of the few world-class players to represent South Africa after its post-apartheid return to international cricket in 1991; and he often carried an otherwise ordinary attack.

Heavy workload

This led to Donald shouldering more than his share of the workload and pressure, leaving him especially vulnerable to injuries. His decision to retire was prompted by the hamstring strain he suffered on the opening day of the first test against Australia in Johannesburg last Friday. The tourists won the test by an innings and 360 runs on Sunday.

Donald will continue to play one-day internationals until the 2003 World Cup, and he said yesterday that he would remain available to the South African team in a non-playing capacity.

Donald bowled with a classical sideways-on action and, until recent seasons, at express pace. His natural aggression was most apparent during his epic duel with England's Mike Atherton at Trent Bridge in 1998, with the latter only finishing on top because an umpire missed a gloved catch to the wicketkeeper.

That series saw Donald at his most devastating: In the five tests, he took 33 wickets at an average of 19.78. He took 20 or more wickets in a series four times.

Post-apartheid

The Afrikaans-speaking Donald came from a working-class Bloemfontein family. At the age of 20, he was selected to represent then-isolated South Africa against a rebel Australian team in 1986-87.

When South Africa returned to the international fold with three one-day internationals against India in 1991, Donald celebrated the occasion in style, taking five for 29 in the first match at Eden Gardens.

He returned match figures of six for 144 on his official test debut, when South Africa returned to test cricket, meeting the West Indies in Barbados in 1992.

Donald made his first-class debut for Free State against Transvaal in 1985-86 and first played for Warwickshire, where he served as overseas professional, in 1987.

In 306 first class matches to date, he has taken 1181 wickets, at an average of 22.68 runs.


Home Print Edition Features
The Bedouin have had enough of roaming
Bedouin tribes are settled on land that they claim is theirs but that the State of Israel still won't officially recognize.
By Ruth Sinai	and Anat Cygielman	| Feb. 28, 2002 | 12:00 AM

Salama Abu Kaf's official residence as stated in his identity card is "the Abu Kaf tribe." His other address is the village of Om Batin, but the state still does not recognize this settlement.

Some 400 families live in the Abu Kaf tribe's settlement, close to the Shoket junction, just one of dozens of Bedouin settlements spread through the northeast Negev. Around 70,000 people live in these settlements, but to the Interior Ministry, they have no known address. They are registered only according to their tribal affiliation. Recent attempts by the Bedouin to register their unrecognized villages as their place of residence have been frequently met with opposition.

The state's refusal to recognize popular centers outside of the seven towns purposely built for Bedouin citizens is not just a symbolic matter - it means that their homes, be it a tent, a shack or a brick house, are considered illegal and are under the constant threat of demolition.

The residents are reluctant to invest in infrastructure or in developing their homes. The houses are not hooked up to the water of electricity networks, and the residents have to improvise: Some connect to the pipes which run across the ground, providing boiling water in summer and ice-cold water in winter. Others draw water from the Mekorot water company service points, while still others rely on the containers system.

They buy generators to provide electricity, but these are run according to financial capability, and usually only in the evenings. In winter, everything leaks - rain falling on the tin roofs produces a deafening noise, and all the access roads are swamped in mud. Burning fires inside the buildings provides warmth, but endangers the residents.

In these no-man's-lands, the schools built by the Education Ministry, some of them brand new, all of them in prefabricated buildings, stand out the most. They are also evidence of the government's contradictory policies - it does not recognize the settlements, and does not provide basic services such as water or electricity, but it does build schools, health and mother and baby clinics, most of them following a High Court of Justice ruling.

The petitions filed in recent years by Bedouin residents of the unrecognized villages at the High Court, with the assistance of human rights organizations, reflect the Bedouin's growing awareness of their civil rights and their refusal to continue living as "non-existent people," as Supreme Court Justice Mishael Cheshin once put it. This awakening is seen particularly in their active involvement in the planning of the area where they live.

The Bedouin have had enough of roaming and now want to set up their own permanent settlements. Signs point visitors to these areas. "Welcome to the Abu Solov clan," reads one on the Be'er Sheva-Dimona road. Mini-markets, cafes and gas stations, all illegal, can be found near the signs.

Part of the reason why the state refuses to recognize these settlements is to pressure them to move to the seven towns set up especially for the Bedouin over the last 30 years. But the harsh lessons learned from the failure of towns like Rahat and Tel Sheva have caused the Bedouin to rebel - now they want to be fully involved in planning the seven new towns approved by the state in 1999 and 2000.

"A Bedouin in the 1980s did not have the tools to deal with the state. He was uneducated. Today, we pit planning against planning, knowledge against knowledge. Knowledge is power - power we use to alter the reality," says Dr Amar Al-Huzayl, the strategic planner of the Regional Council of Unrecognized Villages. The council is a political body set up by the Bedouin in 1996 which claims to recognize some 85 percent of the diaspora population. The council is very active, and deeply involved in discussions on the planning of the new towns.

The Israeli authorities refer to the spontaneous Bedouin settlement outside of existing towns as "a diaspora." The Bedouin meanwhile talk of "unrecognized villages." These definitions come with their own attitudes, and political agenda.

Al-Huzayl wants the state to recognize 45 currently unrecognized villages, scattered mainly around Be'er Sheva and Dimona and Arad. He says the total area of these villages is about 180,000 dunams, which the Bedouin say they own. "The district and national outline plans ignore the Arab population in the Negev, particularly the unrecognized villages," he says. Al-Huzayl wants to see these settlements recognized as villages in the places where they currently stand, and wants to see them under the auspices of one regional council, which would have municipal jurisdiction over 1.153 million dunams of land.

Yaakov Katz, head of the Israel Lands Administration's Bedouin division, rejects this classification. "What is a village? A village has a central road, a public area and a residential area. Most of the diaspora [settlements] are not villages, rather a random distribution of buildings, with no connection between them and with no logic. There are only seven or ten clusters that have signs of a settlement."

Aerial photographs illustrate this, showing enormous lands, dotted with buildings hundreds of meters a part, near groups of houses.

The number of these settlements in a key question. According to Nahum Donsky, who heads the planning team for the Be'er Sheva metropolis master plan, there are 100-150 "centers of significant population with more than 10 buildings." But establishing so many new villages, or even the 45 that Al-Huzayl wants, is impossible. "Land and water are finite resources, agriculture as a financial base is shrinking in the Jewish and Bedouin sectors, and infrastructures cost money. The balance between needs and the possibilities and limits has been found in the planning of the new settlements," says Donsky.

Because of the urgent need for services for the Bedouin community, "service centers" are being built, which will form the core of the future settlements, and help prove to the Bedouin that the government is sincere in its desire to end their plight.

"Every time a ministerial committee, or a committee of directors-general, is set up, they make decisions, but never implement them. Each stage is so complicated, so bureaucratic, and it always seems as if the ministries are there not to help, but to hinder," says Dodik Shoshani, a member of Kibbutz Lahav, who has been following the Bedouin's struggle for rights for decades and who headed the Bedouin Administration in the late 1990s.

Every service center will include schools, kindergartens, a clinic, mosque, sports hall, open-air market, council building, old-age home and employment center. Donsky says that pipes for water system for the centers are already being put into place.

The planners of the new settlements claim that they have learned from the failings of the first group of towns. This time round, there will not be centers of enormous tension between a couple of tribes, but the towns instead will overlap existing settlements, built mostly on land which the residents say is theirs and meant for only one clan. Only the town of Marit will be made up of five "development nucleus," which will each be inhabited by one tribe, separated by agricultural "buffer zones." Each clan, however, demands its own village, and they are currently embroiled in drawn-out discussions with the authorities in an attempt to reach a compromise.

Similar talks are underway which each one of the clans who are earmarked for a village. Katz says that the planning of two towns has reached an impasse "because the residents are not cooperating." The two in question are Mulda, where the tribe has five quareling sects, and Om Batin where the members of the Abu Kaf clan can't decide who represents them.

The dialogue is a long, slow process, and reveals differences of opinion within the Bedouin society - not just between those who may get their own town, but it also raises questions of principles, such as what is a village and what character will the new settlements have? How will the land be divided between the residents? All the while, feuds between the various clans continue.

Al-Huzayl, who wants to see one rural model for each of the 45 villages he demands, wants 30 dunams of land each for 70 percent of the individual families. He believes that this will be enough to meet the natural growth of the population, which he expects to top 200,000 by 2020. Ibrahim Abu Kaf, however, another spokesman for the Abu Kaf clan and the head of the community council of the unrecognized villages, is opposed to this equality, saying that Al-Huzayl has failed to take into account that some families have more land than others. "He thinks families will agree to an equal division, and that is a very big mistake," says Abu Kaf, whose family claim historic ownership of 400 dunams in the area.

Katz claims that the existing towns, the planned extensions and the ones on the way, will house the residents of 38 of the 45 villages Al-Huzayl is demanding. Every one of the new towns is situated in the geographic center of the tribe, but those branches living further off will have to pull up roots and move.

Furthermore, according to Al-Huzayl"the Bedouin Administration is an illegitimate body." He wants to see the responsibility for planning the towns taken out of the administration's hands, which invest NIS 12 million in the projects in 2001.

Alongside the negotiations with the authorities, Al-Huzayl and his planners are putting together alternative plans for settlements, with funding from the New Israel Fund. "If they want the Bedouin to live in this country as citizens, then they have to at least recognize our lands. Moshe from the Moshav sits on state-owned land, but what is state-owned land? The nation's land. And who is the nation? The Jewish people. I do not belong to the Jewish people and so the state-owned land is not mine. How can I be a citizen when I am outside the definition of the nation's land. We are not citizens, but natives. We want to go from natives without land to citizens with a right to land," says Al-Huzayl.

However he emphasizes that, "our struggle is purely civilian. Our successes are also for the state - a democratic country cannot allow itself such a reality of apartheid."


Home Print Edition Opinion
The war's seventh day
Israel's security cannot be based only on the sword; it must rather be based on our principles of moral justice and on peace with our neighbors - those living next door and those living a little further away.
By Michael Ben-Yair	Mar. 3, 2002 | 12:00 AM

The Zionist dream's realization and the Jewish people's national rebirth through the creation of Israel were achieved not because of the Jewish side's superior number of tanks, planes or other aggressive means. The State of Israel was born because the Zionist movement realized it must find a solution to the Jews' persecution and because the enlightened world recognized the need for that solution.

The enlightened world's recognition of the solution's moral justification was an important, principal factor in Israel's creation. In other words, Israel was established on a clear, recognized moral base. Without such a moral base, it is doubtful whether the Zionist idea would have become a reality.

The Six-Day War was forced upon us; however, the war's seventh day, which began on June 12, 1967 and has continued to this day, is the product of our choice. We enthusiastically chose to become a colonial society, ignoring international treaties, expropriating lands, transferring settlers from Israel to the occupied territories, engaging in theft and finding justification for all these activities. Passionately desiring to keep the occupied territories, we developed two judicial systems: one - progressive, liberal - in Israel; and the other - cruel, injurious - in the occupied territories. In effect, we established an apartheid regime in the occupied territories immediately following their capture. That oppressive regime exists to this day.

The Six-Day War's seventh day has transformed us from a moral society, sure of the justice of Israel's creation, into a society that oppresses another people, preventing it from realizing its legitimate national aspirations. The Six-Day War's seventh day has transformed us from a just society into an unjust one, prepared to expand its control atop another nation's ruins. The discarding of our moral foundation has hurt us as a society, reinforcing the arguments of the world's hostile elements and sowers of evil and intensifying their influence.

The intifada is the Palestinian people's war of national liberation. Historical processes teach us that no nation is prepared to live under another's domination and that a suppressed people's war of national liberation will inevitably succeed. We understand this point but choose to ignore it. We are prepared to engage in confrontation to prevent an historical process, although we are well aware that this process is anchored in the moral justification behind every people's war of national liberation and behind its right to self-determination, and although we are well aware that this process will attain its inevitable goal.

This is the background of the difficult testimony we have received about actions of Israel Defense Forces personnel in the occupied territories. No need to repeat the details of the painful phenomena entailed in the occupation regime and in our battle to prolong it. Suffice it to recall the killing of little children fleeing for safety; the executions, without trial, of wanted persons who were not on their way to launch a terrorist act; and the encirclements, closures and roadblocks that have turned the lives of millions into a nightmare. Even if all these actions stem from our need to defend ourselves under an occupation's conditions, the occupation's non-existence would render them unnecessary. Thus, a black flag hovers over these actions.

This is a harsh reality that is causing us to lose the moral base of our existence as a free, just society and to jeopardize Israel's long-range survival. Israel's security cannot be based only on the sword; it must rather be based on our principles of moral justice and on peace with our neighbors - those living next door and those living a little further away. An occupation regime undermines those principles of moral justice and prevents the attainment of peace. Thus, that regime endangers Israel's existence.

It is against this background that one must view the refusal of IDF reservist officers and soldiers to serve in the territories. In their eyes, the occupation regime is evil and military service in the occupied territories is evil. In their eyes, military service in the occupied territories, which places soldiers in situations forcing them to commit immoral acts, is evil, and, according to their conscience, they cannot be party to such acts. Thus, their refusal to serve is an act of conscience that is justified and recognized in every democratic regime. History's verdict will be that their refusal was the act that restored our moral backbone.

The author was attorney general from 1993-96.


Home Print Edition News
After Four Mothers, the Seventh Day arrives
The name Four Mothers Movement was coined on March 4, 1997. Five years to the day, another new movement that also calls for withdrawal - this time from the territories - has been established.
By Ruth Sinai	| Mar. 7, 2002 | 12:00 AM

On March 4, 1997, a name was coined for a protest group whose activities contributed to the historic decision to pull out of Lebanon - the Four Mothers Movement. Zohara Antebi, one of the four, sees symbolic significance in the fact that five years to the day, another new movement that also calls for withdrawal - this time from the territories - has been established.

It is called The Seventh Day - the day that will follow and complete the Six Day War, 35 years after it began.

The comparison with Four Mothers is inevitable, even though only some of the seven signatories to the declaration of intention of the new movement were members of the other protest group. And unlike the motivation of many of the activists in Four Mothers - fear that their sons would die without reason in Lebanon - this time the vision is broader. "Then it was an outburst of anguish. Today it is more the result of a process," Antebi says.

Four Mothers sprang spontaneously from the disaster in which two IDF helicopters on their way to Lebanon crashed, killing 73 soldiers. The Seventh Day is the product of another movement, Mabat L'Ofek (The Vista), set up by Antebi and other Four Mother activists with the aim of having a dialogue with the settlers.

The topic was not withdrawal then, but an attempt to work out together what shape the country's future should take. The dialogue led to a split in Four Mothers and some of those who were opposed to the dialogue are currently organizing into a group called The Fifth Mother, with the aim of withdrawing from the territories. Unlike The Seventh Day, its members are all women and its tone is motherly.

Needless to say, the call for a withdrawal from the territories is not new and different movements have tried to advocate it for years. But because of Four Mothers' reputation as a grassroots movement - largely of women - that succeeded in influencing military and political policy, the new movement is arousing a great deal of excitement.

In the three days since news of the movement got out, support is spreading in leaps and bounds. "It's great," says Antebi while the telephone does not stop ringing in the background. "Four Mothers was small change compared to this."

Local and foreign TV crews, well known and anonymous peace activists, are all contacting the group to hear more. Not all the responses are favorable. On one Web site where the new movement was mentioned, surfers responded by calling them "four terrorists" and there have been threatening telephone calls. They are used to the threats from the Lebanon days.

They say that the "Lebanonization" of the territories dictates a "Lebanonization" in response - namely, a withdrawal more or less to the 1967 borders. But, with that, the comparison ends.

Danny Reshef, a former intelligence officer who was a member of Four Mothers, says Lebanon interested about 3 percent of the population - those who lived in the range of the Katyushas

in the north and those who had sons serving in Lebanon. "Now we are talking about something that touches the very heart and shape of our society," he says. "A leadership that is demanding that the public stand firm must present it with an aim and explain why. We are suggesting an aim and a kind of hope."

The group's aim is a democratic Jewish state that does not occupy another people and does not practice apartheid. The hope is that the terror will one day end, even though Reshef does not delude anyone that it will be immediately or as the result of a withdrawal. But a withdrawal from the territories will make it possible to invest in Israel's social fabric - in development towns, education, in social and economic affairs.

In the same way as Four Mothers broke the taboo of withdrawal from Lebanon and made it a political issue, so The Seventh Day hopes to create a public debate and to give legitimation to expression for a desire to withdraw from the territories.

Members of the movement have come out openly against the group of reserve officers who refuse to serve in the territories and against left-wing activists who see the only key in dialogue with the other side. Antebi says that neither the Lebanese nor the Palestinians, but only the Israelis themselves, can decide on the nature of Israeli society.

The founders, like those of Four Mothers, are all residents of kibbutzim and moshavim in the north. In the Four Mothers this caused a split and the departure of most of the activists from the center of the country. "The movement ends in Haifa," they used to quip.

The rifts were hidden from the public eye so as not to jeopardize the efforts to bring about a withdrawal. The movement's major strength was its ability to gain the attention of the media, including grabbing many headlines that were not always accurate.


Home Business
Balata has fallen
When all is said and done, the army is waging a deluxe war: It is bombing and shelling defenseless cities and villages, and that situation is convenient for both the army and the settlers.
By Ze'ev Sternhell	Mar. 8, 2002 | 12:00 AM

There was something surreal about the television appearance, last Saturday night, by the commanders of the two brigades that operated in the refugee camps of the northern West Bank. The commander of the Paratroop Brigade declared: Balata camp has surrendered. Indeed, the refugee camp was "conquered" by elite forces, using state-of-the-art weaponry, and backed up by tanks, armored personnel carriers and helicopters. If the whole thing were not sad and grotesque, it would be amusing.

But this is a story that is characteristic of the road that has been followed by heroic little Israel, which was admired by the whole Western world, until this terrible period. There were times when the paratroopers were known as the fighters of the Mitla Pass, Ammunition Hill and the Chinese Farm. The Golani Brigade used to be famed for breaching the fortifications of Rafah, as the fighters of Tel el Faher and the Hermon outpost. Their sons and grandsons have fallen to the level of breachers of walls in shacks built of blocks and boards. And they are no longer ashamed to speak of war when what they are really engaged in is colonial policing, which recalls the takeover by the white police of the poor neighborhoods of the blacks in South Africa during the apartheid era.

There was a time - on the first day of the 1967 Six-Day War - when the commander of a tank company in 7th Brigade, Avigdor Kahalani, stopped his tank column in the midst of advance to contact near Rafah so he wouldn't run over two frightened Bedouin children. He waited until their mother came to collect them. Later that day, Kahalani's tank was hit and he suffered extensive burns. To the division commander, Major General Israel Tal, the behavior of the young officer, and not just his fighting, was exemplary.

Today, again in Rafah, army men of a different generation watch as children play next to a booby-trapped bomb that was placed there by the IDF and don't lift a finger. It must have been clear to all of them that if the children touched the bomb it would explode, with loss of life. When the military advocate general finally decided to launch an investigation into the incident - in which five children were killed - the division commander did everything in his power to prevent the probe from taking place.

In colonial Israel, and more especially the Israel in which advocates of "transfer" sit in the government, human life is cheap - and therein lies the most serious danger to our future. A society in which dozens of children are killed as a result of army operations can easily lose its last remaining moral inhibitions. The fact that the Palestinians are also killing indiscriminately cannot absolve us of responsibility for what is going on in the territories. The killing of innocent people is gradually becoming a norm, and that norm is being implemented in the service of a goal that seeks to deprive another people of its freedom and its human rights: The Sharon government is turning the territories into one huge jailhouse, and is turning its citizens in warders who are called upon to suppress a prisoner uprising. That was not quite the purpose of Zionism.

If the army is dominated by shamelessness, and if purely military actions by the Palestinians, such as successful attacks on army outposts and checkpoints, are included under the rubric of terrorism, the settlers' camp is doing all it can to label our inability to cope with the Palestinians' war of independence as the "Rosh Hashanah War." This half-baked attempt to create symmetry between a just war and a campaign of colonialist suppression is not merely a curiosity: It is the desecration of the memory of those who fell in the 1973 Yom Kippur War. It won't be long before we are told that the battle in which tank crews risked their lives on the banks of the Suez Canal and the effort in which an Israeli tank destroys a Palestinian car containing a mother and her three children is the same war.

We should take note here of an interesting phenomenon. The number of Israeli civilian casualties in the past year is far greater than the number of soldiers who have been killed or wounded. When all is said and done, the army is waging a deluxe war: It is bombing and shelling defenseless cities and villages, and that situation is convenient for both the army and the settlers. They are well aware that if the army were to sustain casualties on the same scale as occurred in Lebanon, we would now be on our way out of the territories.
