We perceive the death of civilians in shooting attacks or at the hands of crazed suicide bombers in the heart of our cities, including the extinction of whole families, as a decree of fate or as a kind of act of nature. However, the death of soldiers immediately poses the critical question: What are the goals of the war? For what end are the soldiers being killed? Who sent them to their death? As long as the conscript troops do not pay too heavily, as long as the reservists are not called up in massive numbers to protect and defend the occupation, the question of "why" does not dictate the national agenda.

However, the atmosphere in the country is rapidly approaching the boiling point. More and more people are beginning to understand that the Israeli reprisal operations only engender despair, and despair gives rise to suicide bombers. Today, when the whole political system is paralyzed, it looks as though it will be possible to bring an end to the madness that is raging here, only if people take to the streets en masse and demand an immediate start to negotiations.


Home Life Books
Territorial imperative
"Eretz Yisrael Hashlema: Emuna Vemediniut" ("Greater Israel: Theology and Policy") by Aryeh Naor, Haifa University and Zmora-Bitan, 421 pages, NIS 92
By Yehoshua Porath	Mar. 8, 2002 | 12:00 AM

"Eretz Yisrael Hashlema: Emuna Vemediniut" ("Greater Israel: Theology and Policy") by Aryeh Naor, Haifa University and Zmora-Bitan, 421 pages, NIS 92

In order for a territorial unit to be perceived as complete, it must have a clear and recognizable identity. This identity is usually the product of physical-geographical conditions, i.e., land features that make an area distinct from its surroundings - by being surrounded on all sides by water, for example (as in Britain), or a settled area in a barren desert with a great river cutting through it (like the Nile Valley in Egypt and Sudan).

A territorial unit can also have its own special character without natural features: History can be the determining factor. If an authoritative regime rules a certain region for many generations, and this regime is distinguished by cultural features, no one will dispute its distinctness as a territorial unit. The wholeness of such a territorial unit will be perceived as flawed or threatened in the wake of any change wrought by political or military initiatives within this traditional domain, or any demand to turn back the clock and restore the old order.

This was the argument of the Polish patriots who, in their bid to regain independence, demanded that Poland recover all the land that once belonged to the Kingdom of Poland, including Lithuania and the Ukraine, up to the Dnieper. What mattered in their eyes was the historical connection; the ethnic differences between Lithuanians, Ukrainians and Poles mattered little.

This was also true of the French demand that Alsace-Lorraine be reinstated as part of the republic because these two provinces had belonged to France beginning in 1648. Again, ethnic distinctions were dismissed as insignificant. The Frenchmen who sought a northeastern border along the Rhine during the reign of Napoleon I were motivated by geographical considerations: Such a border would create a natural demarcation, similar to the Mediterranean, the Pyrenees and the Atlantic Ocean.

What is common to all holders of such views is insensitivity to ethnic, linguistic and cultural differences and the desires of the inhabitants themselves. After all, geographical or historical borders do not always coincide with the ethnic reality or the national affiliation of the resident population.

Supporters of the "Greater Land of Israel" approach - or "Israel on both banks of the Jordan," as the Revisionists used to say - cannot lay claim to continuous historical rule over this region, and certainly not natural borders that create a clear distinction between Israel and its neighbors. What they have in their bag is a long history of emotional attachment to ancient Israel, from which the Jews were exiled thousands of years ago, but without a clear territorial definition.

The boundaries of the British Mandate for Palestine became a new factor in the argument. When the victors of World War I divvied up the booty, Great Britain was given the mandate for Iraq and Palestine. In practice, establishing the borders of Iraq, and afterward drawing the line between French rule in Syria and Lebanon, and British rule in the south, produced a map (on paper, at least).

Out of pan-Middle Eastern considerations, however, the British mandatory authorities did not protest when Emir Abdullah of Hejaz seized control of mandated territory that became Transjordan and instated a separate administration in the eastern part of Palestine. Most importantly, he convinced the League of Nations to exclude Transjordan from the area to which the Balfour Declaration applied, thereby closing it to Jewish settlement.

In this way, Palestine was "ripped apart," putting an end to "Greater Israel." The entire Zionist movement, including Jabotinsky, Ussishkin, Tabenkin and Rabbi Berlin of Mizrahi, surrendered in 1922 to the British ultimatum, according to which any objection of the Zionist movement to the cutback in territory would result in Britain's blocking approval of the mandate by the League of Nations. Later, however, all of these Zionist figures expressed their opposition to this territorial "breach" (the "first partition of Palestine"), each in his own style and employing arguments that fit in with his world view.

After resigning from the Zionist Executive in 1923 and founding his own party, the Union of Zionists-Revisionists, Ze'ev Jabotinsky turned this opposition to partitioning Palestine and his insistence on the return of the Jewish people to "both banks of the Jordan," into the foundation stone of radical Zionism, which was presented as an alternative to the conciliatory and conformist approach of the leaders of the Zionist movement, and especially Dr. Chaim Weizmann.

Both the radicals and the realists, it must be said, shared the idea that the Jewish people enjoyed full rights over Palestine on the strength of an emotional and religious attachment of thousands of years. All believed that the opposition of the Arab residents to the return of the Jews was no match for this bond, which was also the source of their right to the land - a right that was confirmed by the British Mandate and the League of Nations. Radicals and realists alike were convinced that the majority of Jews would return. They would do so because the harsh reality in their countries of residence would force them to, even if the Zionist movement was unable to mobilize broad active support at the time (the 1920s and 30s).

If the meteoric rise of the anti-Zionist Bund movement in Poland during the second half of the 1930s seemed to indicate the opposite trend, the Zionist movement could cite Germany as proof that a large Jewish community, assimilated and mostly anti-Zionist, could be forced into altering its traditional approach and embracing Zionism, steadily increasing its participation in Zionist affairs and rate of immigration to Palestine.

The real argument between radicals and realists was not over the matter of right but over capability. The radicals insisted that the Jewish people was capable of turning Palestine into a Jewish homeland. After all, the majority of Jews would move there. The realists understood that this capability was limited, and that not all Jews would settle in Palestine. They realized that the opposition of the Arabs and their national demands would have to be taken into account, and that Britain would be sympathetic to the Arabs because of its multiple interests in other Arab countries.

The conflict between these two camps rose to new heights in the summer of 1937, when an important British body, the Peel Commission, submitted a proposal to divide Palestine into three parts: a Jewish state on 15 percent of the land, a perpetual British mandate over 5 percent (the corridor between Jerusalem and Jaffa) and the annexation of all the rest to Transjordan, which would remain temporarily under British Mandatory control.

Opponents of this proposal in the Labor party were concerned that the territory allotted to the Jewish state would not be large enough to accommodate the millions of Jews they anticipated. Some of them (like Berl Katzenelson) were prepared to consider a more equitable division of territory. Leaders of the Mizrahi movement who opposed the plan objected on the grounds that the Jewish people owned all of the land, which had been promised to them by God. The Revisionists cited historical-legal arguments: the Jews' historical ties to the land, the promises of the Balfour Declaration as a binding contract, and the need for a home for millions of Jews.

In 1937, when radical Revisionists like Uriel Halperin-Shelach - better known as the poet Yonatan Ratosh - realized that Zionist goals could not be achieved under the British, they proposed seizing control of the country by revolution and organizing the immigration of 18 million Jews, whose presence would turn the demographic scales completely. Ratosh, like other Revisionists, was convinced that Arab national resistance was not a source of concern.

The 1947 partition plan aroused less controversy. After what had happened to the Jews in World War II, even a truncated state seemed preferable by far to the continuation of British mandatory rule, with its restrictions on immigration and gradual transfer of the country to the Arabs.

After the conquests of the Six-Day War, the dispute flared once more, this time with greater intensity than ever before. The British component was out of the picture, and the Arab forces had been roundly defeated. So great was the victory, that the religious Zionist public, and even, little by little, the anti-Zionist Haredi community, believed that it was a miracle, a product of divine intervention, and an important step in the direction of messianic redemption, as envisaged by the rabbis Kook, father and son.

But ethnic-geographic reality has proved to be the strongest of them all. Slowly, it has become clear that the Zionist dream cannot be achieved as the Arab population in this region continues to grow. Menachem Begin and the Herut party were forced to give up eastern Transjordan. Today's supporters of a Greater Israel are clenching their teeth and painfully coming to terms with the idea that parts of western Israel will remain under Palestinian Arab control.

Of course, those who are in favor of an apartheid regime and believe they can maintain it in the face of opposition from the enlightened West, and those who believe that one day we will find a way of getting rid of the Arabs, have continued to cultivate the dream of Greater Israel. They are still founding and building up isolated settlements like Netzarim and Tapuah in the heart of Arab territory, thereby imposing a heavy military and economic burden on the State of Israel, and doing it no good politically.

Aryeh Naor has written a fascinating and credible book that analyzes all these developments, explores the various arguments put forward over decades of debate, and exposes their overt or hidden weaknesses. As we read, we are led down the path that Naor himself has traveled, toward acceptance of the idea that this country must be divided between two peoples if our interest in preserving separate ethnic identities takes precedence over our territorial attachment to this jointly claimed stretch of land. Naor's book should be required reading for anyone who has ever voiced an opinion or taken a stand on the ideological dispute still raging among us.

Prof. Yehoshua Porath's biography of Yonatan Ratosh, "Shelah Ve'et Beyado," was published by Zmora-Bitan.


Home Print Edition News
Palestinians charge ahead in British propaganda war
LONDON - While Israelis argue over who will emerge with the upper hand in the Israeli-Palestinian conflict, the Palestinians are busy chalking up impressive achievements in the war of images they have been waging in Britain for a year.
By Sharon Sadeh	Mar. 17, 2002 | 12:00 AM

LONDON - While Israelis argue over who will emerge with the upper hand in the Israeli-Palestinian conflict, the Palestinians are busy chalking up impressive achievements in the war of images they have been waging in Britain for a year.

The gradual erosion in Israel's status in the United Kingdom is, at least in part, due to the vigorous activities of Arab and pro-Palestinian organizations, with the assistance of dozens of British volunteers, who wish to see Israel portrayed as a racist, pariah state. This propaganda campaign is being waged in a number of areas.

The Palestine Solidarity Campaign, a volunteer-based group, is in charge of the political, trade and media arenas. Its Web site www.palestinecampaign.org includes a detailed list of campaigns and events, with a view to raising questions on the legitimacy of Israel's existence, and to encourage embargoes on Israeli produce in Britain, which it calls being "an accomplice to Israel's brutal military occupation of Palestinians."

The site also lists British firms and bodies who have "sinned" by cooperating with the "Zionist tyrant," and the names of Israeli firms operating in Britain.

The Israeli Embassy in Britain claims that the main part of the campaign, entitled Boycott Israeli Goods, has been a virtual failure. Most British businesses have refused to cooperate, despite protests outside supermarkets and department stores, and the distribution of tens of thousands of leaflets.

Two London department stores, Selfridges and Harrods, which initially agreed to take goods produced in the West Bank, Gaza Strip and Golan Heights off their shelves, soon reversed the decision, though they did put up tags stating where in Israel the goods had been produced. Harrods' Egyptian owner, Mohammed Al Fayed, stressed that he had no intention of boycotting Israeli goods, or becoming embroiled in any trade boycotts or disputes, while fully endorsing "the importance of free trade between all countries in the Middle East."

The trade balance between Israel and Britain - Israel's third largest export market in the European Union - shows, however, that there has been a 15 percent fall in trade between the two countries, from $4.073 billion in 2000 to $3.439 billion in 2001. In addition, there has been a 30 percent fall in tourism from Britain, and these trends are expected to continue.

Israeli representatives tend to put the above down to the economic slowdown in both countries coupled with the worsening security situation in Israel, though they do admit that Israeli companies are finding it harder and harder to operate in the British market.

Talks between the Israeli wine producer Yarden and a large supermarket chain fell through, apparently over financial, rather than political, differences. British companies who operate or invest in Israel have refused to have their logos included in Israeli public relations films, fearing that this could damage their business in Britain and other countries.

Ha'aretz has also learned of cases where senior Jewish officials in British firms have hesitated and sometimes even avoided contacting and cooperating with Israeli companies.

Britain's Department of Trade and Industry may have declared Israel a "target market" because of its purchase potential, but it is stalling on issuing export licenses for selling military components to Israel. In addition, the Foreign Office recently ordered Israel to explain why it had modified British Centurion tanks into armored personnel carriers, despite a written assurance from Israel in November 2000 that no British component was being used as part of the army's activities in the territories. Palestinians, with the assistance of British Members of Parliament, are now trying to thwart the sale of anti-tank artillery produced by Rafael, the Israel Armament Development Authority, claiming that the missiles had been used against the Palestinians during the current uprising.

The pro-Palestinian groups' achievements are even more impressive in the field of education. Israeli students and lecturers were shocked to discover recently that one of the leading colleges at Oxford, St. Anthony's, had allowed student members of the Arab Cultural Society to invite Joseph Massad of Columbia University to the college some two weeks ago to give his lecture "On Zionism and Jewish Supremacy."

Massad claimed in his lecture that the "Jews are not a nation" and that "a Jewish state is a racist state" that does not have the right to exist. Oxford University looked into the matter following a number of complaints, and found that although Massad's comments were certainly controversial, it was legitimate to voice them. The university admitted that it had "reservations about the title, which implied reference to the "Protocols of the Elders of Zion."

A motion which states that anti-Zionism is not anti-Semitism and that Israel is an apartheid state was recently tabled at students union meetings around the country, a joint-initiative of ultra left-wingers, and Palestinian and Muslim activists. It was passed by the University of London's School of Oriental and African Studies' Student Union, which also prohibited the sale of Israeli goods on campus.

A similar motion won a majority at the University of Manchester Student Union, but did not pass as it did not have the required two-thirds majority. The rejection of the motion at a university where some 600 Jewish students are enrolled, was reportedly met by a wave of anger and frustration by Muslims and Palestinians, with Jewish students being attacked, bricks thrown at Hillel House, the Jewish student digs, and a knife was thrust into the door of a Jewish student's dormitory room.

The anti-Israel wave currently washing over Britain is leaving its mark. Almost all events aimed at increasing understanding between Jews and Muslims have been called off, and a similar fate has befallen Israeli-Palestinian initiatives. Jewish communities security personnel report a steady rise in assault attempts and vandalism on Jews and synagogues, and many Jews are now concerned that a significant escalation in violence between Israel and the Palestinians, or even a U.S.-led attack on Iraq, could even lead to attempted murder.


Home Life Books
Days of Ubuntu
It all began at Bergen-Belsen The first two people I spoke with about national reconciliation in South Africa were murderers; the third is the current minister of education. "When did you first hear the term Ubuntu," I asked Ntobeko Penni, who answered that he'd heard his father use it when he was six years old. "Ubuntu" - humanness in Zulu - is an African outlook on life, the essence of which is that a person is a person through other people. Some say this perspective on life is largely
By Tom Segev	and in Cape Town	| Mar. 26, 2002 | 12:00 AM

It all began at Bergen-Belsen

The first two people I spoke with about national reconciliation in South Africa were murderers; the third is the current minister of education. "When did you first hear the term Ubuntu," I asked Ntobeko Penni, who answered that he'd heard his father use it when he was six years old. "Ubuntu" - humanness in Zulu - is an African outlook on life, the essence of which is that a person is a person through other people. Some say this perspective on life is largely what led to the foundation of the Truth and Reconciliation Commission (TRC), after apartheid's collapse in the latter half of the 1990s.

Penni, 28, was one of four young black men who murdered Amy Biehl, a young American woman who came to South Africa to show her solidarity with the black struggle. Penni and his comrades attacked her in the street and tore her to shreds.

Education Minister Kader Asmal said he only heard of Ubuntu in the past few years, after the term began to be used by the media as part of an attempt to root the TRC in the African tradition.

Asmal is a short, mustachioed man of 68, a Muslim of Indian descent, who looks and sounds like a British professor. No wonder - a graduate of the London School of Economics, he was not permitted to return to his homeland, and spent the next 25 years teaching law at Trinity College in Dublin, Ireland. Asmal was born in Natal province; his father was a shopkeeper. When Asmal was 11, he told me, he sneaked into a whites-only cinema. It was 1945. He doesn't remember what film was shown, but the newsreel that day changed his life. It was about the British Army's arrival at the Bergen-Belsen concentration camp. He has never forgotten, and never will, the images of bulldozers clearing away piles of bodies, said Asmal. Afterward, he hurried to tell his mother that despite her wishes, he would not be an imam, but that he wanted to study law, so he could help those subjected to political persecution. And so he did. His legal and political activities led to his exile.

While living in Britain and Ireland, said Asmal, he spent a lot of time studying the history of Nazi Germany, the annihilation of the Jews and the Nuremberg trials. The more he learned, the more he expanded his political activity, and became one of the chief spokesmen in Europe against apartheid. One dream kept him going: Someday the evil regime in South Africa would collapse, and he would return home and devote himself to bringing to trial the criminals of the white regime; it would be an African Nuremberg trial.

In September 1990, Asmal was permitted to return. He accepted a university post as a professor of human rights, and began preparing for the trials he'd been envisioning for all those years. But Oliver Tambo, the legendary leader of the African National Congress (ANC), the leading underground group, decided there would be no trials in South Africa akin to those held for Nazi criminals, as such trials were vindictive, and were liable to tear up South Africa instead of uniting and building it. South Africa needed reconciliation, not revenge, he said. Other people involved in the establishment of the TRC confirm Asmal's story - up to here. Nor is there any argument that Tambo's and especially Nelson Mandela's wisdom and forethought, prevented Nuremberg-style "trials of revenge."

According to Asmal, Tambo asked him to think of another way to expose the truth about the crimes of the white regime. After some thought, Asmal suggested that instead of a trial, the government set up a public commission of inquiry. At first they wanted to call it the "Truth Commission," but in order to prevent any critics from dwelling on the Orwellian origins of the name, they settled on "Truth and Reconciliation Commission." The idea was implemented after much discussion, to which many voices contributed. One was attorney Albie Sachs, another champion of human rights.

Mama said

Sachs, of Jewish descent, is now 67. He was arrested during the apartheid era for his activities on behalf of the ANC. He was held in isolation and eventually forced out of his country. At first he settled in England, but later moved to Mozambique, where as a law professor he continued his anti-apartheid activities. The South African authorities must have felt that he had strong influence; they dispatched secret agents to Mozambique, who planted a bomb in his car. Sachs was miraculously saved, but the intensity of the blast caused him to lose his right arm and one eye. He, too, went home in 1990, and he now serves as a justice of the Constitutional Court.

The Truth and Reconciliation Commission, Sachs told me, was not born so as to settle accounts with the white regime, but as the result of the need to deal with crimes of the ANC itself. The story begins with a session of the ANC's National Executive Committee in August 1993, held about eight months before the first-ever democratic elections in South Africa. It was a difficult meeting. The issue was how to deal with a report of an internal commission set up by the ANC to expose the barbaric interrogation methods used by its members against collaborators with the apartheid regime. Aside from the horrific tortures administered during interrogations, ANC men would detain suspected collaborators, place tires around their necks, and set them aflame. Sachs is proud that the ANC made the decision to investigate itself. It may be the only liberation movement to ever have done so, he says.

However, the feeling of self-satisfaction was not enough; the findings were atrocious, and something clearly had to be done. Discussion of the report continued for a long time. It was embarrassing, as the ANC was poised to assume control and lead South Africa into a new era. Finally, one of the participants stood up and asked simply, "Let us think for a moment what my mama would say about it." When he said `my mama' he meant the healthy logic of a simple, unsophisticated woman who may be unaware of politics but is blessed with a robust sense of decency and justice.

The speaker then offered the answer: "We are simply crazy. Here we are, examining our own weaknesses and faults, and exposing our nakedness to the whole world - which maybe has to be done - while in the meantime those rascals and villains on the other side, who for decades and centuries murdered, mutilated and tortured our people, are getting away scot-free." The others nodded their heads, and then Kader Asmal proposed a truth commission, which would expose all of the evil in its entirety, of all the evildoers, whites and non-whites alike.

Previously, there had been much discussion in South Africa about the possibility of setting up a forum for clarifying the historic truth. Committees had been formed, surveys held, experts from other countries consulted. There was a study of how other countries, such as Argentina, Chile and Zimbabwe, had handled the issue.

The upheaval

The apartheid regime evolved over the years and did not collapse all at once. It gradually crumbled, primarily, it seems, as a result of the international sanctions enacted against South Africa. The breakup of the Soviet Union nullified the main factor that justified the apartheid regime, besides the racist ideology itself. Many whites truly believed that the evil regime was necessary to protect their country from the Communist threat. In retrospect, everyone now speaks of ideological laxity, fatigue, a general feeling of "we can't go on this way" among the whites. The governing party had become a doormat, the economic situation deteriorated. There was a feeling that everything was falling apart, and masses of people were leaving the country.

In the 1980's, the authorities began to cancel some regulations of "the petty apartheid," which forbade blacks from sitting in public parks on white-only benches, or using telephone booths or public restrooms designated for use by whites. However, the fundamentals of discrimination, including the lack of a parliamentary vote for blacks, were supposed to remain unchanged. But the willingness to nibble at the foundations of apartheid seems to have done to South Africa what perestroika did to the Soviet Union. The authorities were unable to control the narrow cracks that opened in the wall of repression, the cracks grew wider and the walls fell. The journalist and commentator Vessel de Kock explained to me that as more black workers were being allowed to live in close proximity to white-owned factories, over time a mixed economy developed: The political system was no longer suited to the economic situation and therefore had to be changed. Meanwhile, the ANC was growing stronger.

Colin Eglin, a veteran parliamentarian, told me that Frederik Willem de Klerk, the last white president, decided to hold talks with the blacks shortly before he lost everything. Nelson Mandela accepted the offer shortly before he was to win everything. The result was a uniquely realistic compromise. Many whites in South Africa, if not all, and numerous foreign observers, assumed that with the collapse of apartheid, the blacks would rise up, drive out the whites, and slaughter any that stayed behind, to avenge centuries of racism, during which the evil was, at its height, equivalent to that of the Nazis. That this did not happen is referred to by some as "the miracle of South Africa."

I spent some time asking people to explain this "miracle" to me, but I eventually stopped. The question itself shows a prejudice, journalist Zubeida Jaffer told me. We aren't a primitive tribe of man-eaters. She suggested I read the ANC publications dating back to its inception in the early 20th century, and especially the freedom charter it adopted in the early `50s. The movement had never threatened to kill the whites. On the contrary, it had always called for reestablishing life on the basis of full equality, calling for South Africa to be a state of all its citizens, blacks and whites and all the others too.

Jaffer is a well-known political commentator. We sailed to Robben Island, where Mandela was held prisoner for 18 of the 27 years of his incarceration. On the way there, she spoke of her own arrest and torture. As a talented reporter with good sources, she was able to report exclusive news about the black struggle. The authorities suspected her of belonging to an underground organization, and had her arrested. The interrogators threatened to rape her - but did not do so, although they did beat her, and forced her to swallow a drug that caused nausea, and pain all over her body. At the time of her second arrest, she was pregnant. The interrogators threatened to "burn her fetus," but she was released before her daughter's birth. Only recently has she begun to recover. Prior to her arrest, Jaffer was allowed to visit prisoners held on Robben Island together with Mandela. The current visit to the prison as a tourist, with me, provoked strange feelings.

Island of Good Hope

Robben Island has served as a place of detention almost from the day Vasco de Gama dropped anchor there 500 years ago, as a quarantine camp for slaves and lepers, and a prison for criminal convicts and political opponents. Some of the men imprisoned together with Mandela now serve as guides, sharing their memories with the tourists. At the height of the tour, the visitor arrives at Mandela's cell.

Conditions were harsh, and the wardens abusive. Mandela and his comrades spent part of the time working in a nearby quarry; the dazzling light severely damaged his eyesight. The long years on Robben Island had an effect on his worldview: the prolonged struggle for improving prison conditions made him more acutely realistic, taught him to better assess what can and cannot be achieved, judge when to be stubborn and when to yield.

The more the whites withdrew from their positions, the more the security forces demanded that any settlement be conditioned upon a blanket amnesty for anyone involved in the crimes of apartheid, including expulsions, liquidations, faked terrorist attacks, torture, murder, rape, plunder and corruption. At one stage, the talks nearly broke down over this demand. Several army leaders threatened to start a civil war. On this point, the nation's jurists, including Justice Minister Dullah Omar, seem to have played a significant role. Albie Sachs' wisdom and prestige also aided in resolving this disagreement.

A blanket amnesty was out of the question, of course, Sachs explained to me. But the controversy threatened the acceptance of the new constitution, which may be the best and most righteous constitution in the world. Its acceptance saved South Africa from a civil war and perhaps from complete destruction. Sachs feels the constitution was worth conceding on the sensitive issue of granting amnesty to apartheid criminals. After much effort, the sides reached a compromise: no blanket amnesty - instead, separate discussion of each and every case as part of the TRC hearings. Along with granting amnesty, the commission was also authorized to rule on compensation to victims of the regime.

Amnesty seekers had to comply with two main conditions: First, they had to disclose the truth about their crimes, although they were not required to express remorse. Second, they had to prove that their acts were performed as part of their political position. This was new: Most people accused of crimes against humanity defend themselves by arguing that "they were only carrying out orders." This line of defense has not been accepted, partially because of the duty of refusing a blatantly illegal order. In South Africa, they took the opposite course: Anyone who could prove he committed the acts while serving the country, or even while serving its political ideology, qualified for amnesty. Many of those harmed by apartheid, especially the families of its victims, had a hard time accepting the idea of criminals going unpunished. Sachs is not happy with the amnesty arrangement, either, even though he had much influence on its acceptance.

Sachs told me about the day that a man named Henry appeared in his chambers. A former South African army officer, he told Sachs that he had been involved in gathering the intelligence prior to the booby-trapping of Sachs' car. Sachs tried to draw him out: He was an excellent officer who evidently believed he was dispatching a dangerous enemy. They sat together for nearly two hours. "He looked at me almost with a measure of jealousy. I was a judge of the Constitutional Court, and he had been cast aside and was unemployed. He too had injuries, he told me. He had been shot in the leg and walked with a slight limp. We could have gone on eyeing each other and talking forever. I stood up and said: `Henry, (a cheap emotion surged in me. I was tempted to say: `I cannot shake your hand. You know why.') I cannot shake your hand now. Go to the TRC, tell them your story, do something for South Africa and then maybe we'll meet again.' He looked so uncomfortable, uneasy and sad when he left."

Henry is one of thousands of witnesses who have appeared before the commission and requested amnesty. The two men eventually did see each other again, at which time Sachs shook the other's hand. Sachs did not ask if Henry had received amnesty. He thinks he did. The number of amnesty recipients is relatively low: about 2,000 of the 7,000 who applied for it. The granting of amnesty has not added much luster to the commission's prestige, and in retrospect, along with its successes it has had its weaknesses. Mary Burton, a member of the commission, told me about them.

Ben-Eliezer's true thoughts
A number of the leaders of Peace Now who walked out of Benjamin Ben-Eliezer's office last Friday had to remind themselves that they had just met with the defense minister.
By Akiva Eldar	| Mar. 26, 2002 | 12:00 AM

A number of the leaders of Peace Now who walked out of Benjamin Ben-Eliezer's office last Friday had to remind themselves that they had just met with the defense minister - the man who bears supreme responsibility for the actions of the Israel Defense Forces and the settlers in the territories - and not the left-wing opposition leader, who is opposed to such actions.

Attending Friday's meeting were former minister Prof. Yuli Tamir, a number of the founders of Peace Now and members of its management: Prof. Arie Arnon; Prof. Dan Jacobson; Elon Portugali and Yair Yanov; the secretary of the National Kibbutz Movement, Gavri Bargil; the director of Peace Now, Moria Shlomot; her predecessor, Gabi Lasky; and the organization's spokesman, Didi Remez.

The meeting was held under the shadow of the new Settlement Sites report compiled by the organization and published the day before in the press. The bureau of the defense minister vehemently denied that since Ben-Eliezer had taken office, no less than 34 new settlement outposts had been established in the West Bank. The minister reiterated his claim that he had given "an unequivocal order" that no mobile home could move without his authorization.

The guests laid out aerial photographs of the settlement sites on the table. Ben-Eliezer had a quick look and then shrugged his shoulders. "It's all marginal. When you are being shot at from 14 different directions, you have to focus on the primary objective," he declared. "I have more important fronts with the settlers."

To appease his guests, Ben-Eliezer asked one of his military aides to look into the matter and to report back to them with his findings as soon as possible.

The minister was clearly trying to find favor in the eyes of his visitors. He knows that if, on election day, these people were to move their protest banners to the courtyard of the Labor Party headquarters, his next boss would be opposition leader Yossi Sarid. According to Yossi Beilin, certain surveys show that a party that unites all the left-wing forces has a pretty good chance of outstripping Ben-Eliezer's party.

The defense minister made an effort, at first, to explain the "cleansing" of the refugee camps to his left-wing guests. He said that the principal terror-makers were concentrated in these camps. The left-wing guests reminded him that various IDF sources were claiming that the incursions into Area A only boosted terrorism.

The defense minister did not contradict these statements, nor did he argue with the forecast that the government would soon have to pass on the "irrelevant" title from Yasser Arafat to Marwan Barghouti. Some security officials, both Israelis and Westerners, are claiming that, following the "cleansing" operation, the status of the Tanzim leader in the territories is enough to already make him worthy to be declared a non-partner by the government.

Ben-Eliezer hinted that the incursions into the refugee camps and towns of the West Bank had been nothing more than a show for domestic purposes. "The neighbors keep telling me that we should slaughter them all," the Labor Party leader confessed to his guests. And someone who wants to be prime minister certainly has to take the neighbors into account.

Prof. Tamir, Ben-Eliezer's most senior Labor Party colleague at the meeting, refused to comment on the defense minister's statements, saying that the participants had agreed not to publicize the contents of the talks. She added she hoped that such an agreement would not undermine the promise to review the claims with regard to the illegal settlement outposts.

Ben-Eliezer prefers Netanyahu

The defense minister agreed that the government of which he is a senior member has no set path, aside from the one dictated to it by the settlers. He confirmed that he was unaware of Prime Minister Ariel Sharon having any political plan. If it had been up to him, he said, he would accept the Saudi initiative and begin negotiations in accordance with the Clinton outline.

Ben-Eliezer also spoke of Palestinian leaders who were quietly telling him that the right of return was no longer an obstacle to an agreement that would be based on the principle of two states for two nations.

"So what are you doing in this government?" the left-wing guests asked and Ben-Eliezer was ready with a response. "I want to bring about implementation of the Tenet plan and the Mitchell report," he said. "Immediately thereafter, we will have to resign because the government won't get further than that."

In contrast, in closed discussions during which he appears to divulge what he truly thinks, Foreign Minister Shimon Peres expresses expectations from Sharon that do not reach implementation of the Mitchell report, which involves a freeze on settlements and the beginning of negotiations toward a permanent agreement. Peres' favorite excuse for staying glued to the government still remains the threat that Benjamin Netanyahu will return to power.

But Netanyahu doesn't scare Ben-Eliezer. On a number of occasions, the defense minister has said that, based on surveys at his disposal, his problem is in fact Sharon. The center, which has an aversion to the old and extreme Netanyahu, finds it hard to believe that the former prime minister has changed and, despite everything, it prefers Sharon.

Whatever the case may be, the working assumption of Labor's leadership is that it holds the key to the date of the coming elections. According to information in the hands of Labor, National Union-Yisrael Beiteinu and the National Religious Party have reached an understanding with Netanyahu that if Ben-Eliezer and his colleagues were to call it a day, the right-wing leaders would not assume the formers' places in the vacant Volvos.

Eitam didn't show

The review with Brig. Gen. (res.) Effi Eitam was supposed to have taken place Sunday night with the head of the National Security Council, Major General Uzi Dayan, who also serves as chairman of the Forum for National Responsibility. Dayan also invited Prof. Yuli Tamir, a member of the forum and head of the Rabin Center. Toward the end of last week, Tamir, one of the initiators of the Kinneret Covenant, asked Dayan to publicly announce that Eitam had disqualified himself from being among the signers of the covenant and thus to "drive out the evil from among us."

Tamir wrote to Dayan that the statements voiced by Eitam in an interview to the Ha'aretz weekend supplement blatantly contradicted the covenant to which he had set his hand. "While the covenant declares a commitment to equal rights for Israel's Arab citizens and recognizes their cultural, language and national rights," Tamir wrote, "Mr. Eitam declares that `Israel's Arabs are, to a certain degree, ticking bombs,' the dangers of which are like the dangers of a cancer eating away at the body of the state, and that they have become a fifth column."

Such statements, which contradict the contents of the covenant and the spirit of equality on which it was based, Tamir continued, disqualified Eitam from being among the signers of the document. She reminds Dayan that when she formulated the covenant, she and her colleagues agreed that any individual who was prepared to accept its principles and sign it would be able to do so. Now, however, she believes that Eitam's statements have made his signature worthless.

In light of such, Tamir is asking Dayan to report to all members of the forum that in her opinion, "If they do not take determined steps to remove Eitam from among them, they are likely to come across as seeing [Eitam's] positions as positions that fit well with the values of the covenant."

Eitam did not show up for the meeting, presumably in protest against criticism launched at him by Tamir a few hours earlier on television. Tamir's gauntlet has been thrown down at the feet of the signatories, most of whom are enlightened and sensitive individuals. They must decide if their signatures to lines such as "all persons were created in the image of God" and "each citizen is equal to all others" can rest alongside the signature of a man who equates a significant number of the citizens of Israel with a cancer.

Anat Gov, who also signed the covenant, said yesterday that after speaking with most of the 60 members of the forum, she had been able to determine that they were averse to Eitam's statements and had deemed him unrepresentative of its signatories. Nevertheless, Gov said, the one to explain to the world and himself how his statements in the interview corresponded with his signing of the covenant was Eitam.

Gov added that the decision whether to remove Eitam from the forum would be taken in light of his response to the comments of its members. The Rabin Center for Israel Studies, which has taken the forum under its wing, is currently witnessing a stormy debate over how to treat an individual whose messianic messages contradict Rabin's heritage. The final decision, so it appears, will rest with Dayan.

From immoral to illegal

More than 30 years have passed since Prof. Yeshayahu Leibowitz ruled that service in the occupied territories was immoral. Now, his grandson, attorney Shamai Leibowitz, has determined that it is also illegal. He has been distributing among conscripts and reservists a legal opinion, under the heading "The Obligation to Refuse," that determines that the very order to serve in the territories is illegal and that the Military Jurisdiction Law exempts all soldiers from obeying the order.

Leibowitz bases his opinion on a 1957 ruling (the Melinki affair), according to which a military directive that involves harming civilians, causing damage to their property, the withholding of medical treatment and the like is totally invalid as an order. In 1957, the court ruled that "The constitution of the State of Israel" forbids executions without trial or the destruction of the facilities and infrastructures of a civilian population. Furthermore, the 1957 ruling determines that an order is illegal if it constitutes a contravention of an international law or an undermining of fundamental moral principles.

Leibowitz quotes from a book, "Tsedek Ba'mishpat," written by retired Supreme Court justice Haim Cohen and published, incidentally, by the Defense Ministry publication house: "The positivist theory according to which `a law is law, an order is an order,' and no one has the right to question them, served tyrannical, fascist and totalitarian legislators as a large tree on which to hang the laws of oppression and exploitation, persecution and discrimination, plundering and robbing, that were the heartfelt desires of absolute rulers."

In the face of the standard argument that in a democratic state, one must obey the orders of the army even if they appear immoral, Leibowitz contends there is no democracy, but only an apartheid regime in the territories.

Leibowitz is convinced his legal opinion will serve as a shield for public figures who publicly call for a refusal to serve in the territories. He argues the IDF captains know they have no way of dealing with his opinion and are, therefore, shying away from a conflict on the matter.

Virtual managers

A few weeks ago already, the spokesman for the Israel Broadcasting Authority confirmed to me that Amnon Nadav, the so-called manager of the Voice of Israel, is not even authorized to ask his secretary for a cup of coffee or to send his driver around the corner. As is the case with the managers of the Arabic radio and television stations, Nadav's contract also expired many months ago and he is being held in place only by his political ties.

The reform proposal of MK Ra'anan Cohen ended with the resume of the minister responsible for the IBA. Instead of the IBA being nursed back to health, the spoils are continuing to be divided between the Prime Minister's Office and the right-wing representatives on the IBA's management committee. There, no one seems very impressed with the Vardi Report, which advised - already a few years ago - replacing the IBA's management, including the manager of the Voice of Israel. The accountants' report on advertising services at the IBA was not very complimentary toward Nadav either.

The politicians have learned that senior officials whose contracts are up in the air tend to develop a marked dependency on the government. As an example, Voice of Israel reporters present Nadav's decision to strip Nili Amir - seen by the former as a "lefty" - of her position as permanent editor of the station's midday news magazine program.

It is unclear why serious journalists take any notice whatsoever of virtual managers.

By the way, Nadav sent his secretary to edit a talk show and he sent his driver to present a song program.

In Ramallah, visiting writers stress the power of words
At a press conference yesterday, members of the delegation spoke of the need to be exact with words, of awareness and of the need for intellectuals to intervene. The delegation had come to visit Palestinian poet Mahmoud Darwish, one of the parliament's founders, and other intellectuals.
By Amira Hass	| Mar. 26, 2002 | 12:00 AM

At a press conference yesterday, members of the delegation spoke of the need to be exact with words, of awareness and of the need for intellectuals to intervene. The delegation had come to visit Palestinian poet Mahmoud Darwish, one of the parliament's founders, and other intellectuals.

"The masters of words have no need for rhetoric before the eloquence of blood," Darwish said. "Therefore our words will be as simple as our rights: We were born on this land and of this land. We knew no other mother, nor any mother-tongue but its own. And when we realized that it has too much history and too many prophets, we understood that pluralism is an all-embracing space and not a prison cell, and that no one has a monopoly over land or God or memory."

Darwish returned recently from Amman and the writers will no doubt find that the Jordanian capital is more accessible - to those who have the money - than Nablus or Bethlehem, and much closer for Palestinians than East Jerusalem.

IPW president Russell Banks, from the United States, spoke of the "corrupted and abused" language used to describe the situation. He said the writers would try to correct this language when they returned home.

Juan Goytisolo of Spain said that Israel was employing state terrorism. We have come to show solidarity with the Palestinians living in ghettoes, he said.

Breyten Breytenbach, who spent eight years in jail for opposing apartheid in South Africa, was asked by a Palestinian journalist if he did not fear he would be termed anti-Semitic. "We have to reject this kind of cultural terrorism that sees every criticism of Israeli crimes against the Palestinians as an expression of anti-Semitism," he replied.

Nobel Prize-winner Jose Saramago of Portugal said that, before his visit to Ramallah he knew there were Israeli tanks there and that PA head Yasser Arafat had been confined to his offices. "I didn't know that two colonies with army posts overlook Ramallah," he said. "I didn't know that every Palestinian settlement is under a separate siege." He said that "the spirit of Auschwitz" was linked to what was going on in Ramallah. "This place is being turned into a concentration camp," he said.

Asked by Ha'aretz where the gas chambers were, he replied; "So far, there are none." He added that, as a writer, it was his prerogative to make emotional comparisons in order to shock people into understanding.

IPW executive director Christian Salmon later told Ha'aretz that the comparison did not reflect the views of the parliament.

Today the IPW delegation will tour the Gaza Strip where the siege is even more obvious than in Ramallah.

Days of Ubuntu - part 2
We are all guilty She was born in Argentina and came to South Africa with her husband, whom she met while studying in Europe. It was the early 1960's. Her husband and his family opposed apartheid and voted for the progressive opposition, but like many other whites, they thanked God that their party was not winning the elections, says Burton. She is not cynical by nature, but the years have taught her to delve behind the slogans and cliches; an impressive woman. She volunteered for a chari
Mar. 26, 2002 | 12:00 AM

We are all guilty

She was born in Argentina and came to South Africa with her husband, whom she met while studying in Europe. It was the early 1960s. Her husband and his family opposed apartheid and voted for the progressive opposition, but like many other whites, they thanked God that their party was not winning the elections, says Burton. She is not cynical by nature, but the years have taught her to delve behind the slogans and cliches; an impressive woman. She volunteered for a charity organization operating in the black townships. The more she struck roots in her new country, the more questions she had. At one stage, she found herself among the women of the "Black Sash," a sort of South African version of Women in Black. The two organizations are in contact with one another. It was legal, extra-parliamentary activity. She was arrested only once, briefly. The Black Sash women opposed the granting of amnesty to apartheid criminals.

This, in fact, caused Burton to rethink acceptance of her appointment to the 17-member commission, who were selected by President Nelson Mandela from a list of candidates who were approved after a hearing that was broadcast on television. None were politicians; most were former anti-apartheid activists. It is not the sort of appointment that is easily turned down, and Mary Burton eventually overcame her hesitancy.

It was a difficult experience. Members of the commission were divided into three sub-committees: one heard evidence of gross human rights violations from 1960 onward; a second handled amnesty requests, and a third dealt with applications for aid to victims and their families, including financial aid and compensation. The hearings went on for two years; the commission heard thousands of witnesses who were first classified by a staff of investigators. A sum of $20 million was allocated to the commission. The testimony they heard was harrowing and heartrending.

At one point or another, every member of the commission reached his or her breaking point, Mary Burton said. She reached hers when hearing a rather ordinary story: A boy of 15 had gone to a grocery store, walked into the crossfire at a demonstration, and was killed. His mother told the story to the commission, while the father sat and looked on. One commission member asked him what he would say to his son's murderers if he met them. Burton feels the question was improper, but the result was almost too hard to bear. Because the father suddenly stood up and gave vent to a colossal outburst of pain and hatred and mainly of helplessness. She does not know why this story is the one that broke her.

The commission was headed by Archbishop Desmond Tutu. He designed its hearings to be a purifying and corrective religious experience. His deputy was Dr. Alex Boraine, a Methodist minister and former MP who now teaches law in New York. Mary Burton said she and several other commission members had some serious arguments with Tutu. They objected to the religious dimension he placed on the hearings. They asked him not to appear at commission hearings wearing his priestly vestments, or to open sessions with a daily prayer. But Tutu, a Nobel Peace Prize laureate, stood his ground. It may have been more than a mere matter of style: The entire character of the proceedings, and especially the idea of amnesty being issued by the commission in exchange for revelation of the truth, was deeply rooted in Christian theology.

Often there was something superficial about the proceedings: All it took was for an individual to confess the truth and he was assured of forgiveness. It is doubtful whether this could have worked as simply between Jews and Muslims. In fact, the conflict in South Africa was resolvable because, unlike the Israeli-Palestinian conflict, it contained no elements of religious rivalry. Most residents of South Africa are Christians, and Tutu used Christianity as a source of inspiration for reconciliation.

Another source of inspiration was the doctrine of Ubuntu. There are still arguments over what had a greater effect on the character of the hearings - Christianity, Ubuntu or politics. The hearings about crimes committed by the black organizations, including those of blacks against blacks, created a "we are all guilty" atmosphere. When everyone is guilty, no one is really guilty, and some critics felt that Tutu seemed to be wrapping his purple robes around everyone. He, on the other hand, saw it as a requisite basis for national reconciliation.

Tutu often sobbed as he listened to the harrowing stories. From time to time he would also call for dramatic gestures of reconciliation. One of the heights of this came as Winnie Mandela completed her testimony. She had been called in to clarify certain facts about the death of a boy named Stompie. He was murdered at her home, evidently with her active and sadistic participation. After her testimony, Tutu suddenly called on the boy's mother to come to the front of the hall, and asked her to embrace Winnie Mandela. He had given her no prior warning. The mother, a simple woman, did as she was asked. It was a big show of reconciliation. Mary Burton was not fond of these scenes. She could barely believe her ears when I told her about my meeting with the murderers of Amy Biehl.

Amy Biehl was here

Hanging in the entry to the central Cape Town offices of the Amy Biehl Foundation is a large photograph in which she is smiling happily, a black child in her arms. Amy, her parents told me, was raised on anti-apartheid; even as a child in California she would wear hats adorned with the slogan "Free Nelson Mandela." It was therefore quite natural for her to visit South Africa as an adult. A graduate of Stanford University, she arrived as a Fulbright scholar and took on all sorts of communal projects. Working with the blacks in South Africa was a reflection of her world view: she believed in the equality of human beings and in multi-racial democracy.

Her four black killers attacked her with stones and knives - they shattered her skull and mutilated her body practically beyond recognition. Peter and Linda Biehl - rather wealthy people, not especially religious - decided to continue her life's work. The foundation they established underwrites education, health and professional training projects, as well as artistic and musical activities - all among blacks, all in the spirit of Amy.

Her four murderers were arrested, tried and sentenced to 18 years in prison. Linda Biehl was present at several court sessions. She says she felt nothing as she sat across from her daughter's murderers. Albie Sachs told her it would be good if she could make some sort of contact with the father of one of the murderers, who was also sitting in the court. At one stage, their eyes met, and at that moment she felt a sadness: He too was about to lose a son, she thought. They shook hands.

The murderers later applied to the TRC and asked that the lynch be recognized as a political act. This was the first step on the road to amnesty. They explained that before committing the murder, they had attended a gathering of a radical black group. The speaker had instructed his listeners to kill any white who happened to cross their path. Amy Biehl happened to be the one. When Peter and Linda Biehl heard of the amnesty request, they called Tutu from their home in California and said they had no objections to granting the amnesty, but that they wanted to be at the hearing. "We wanted South Africa to know that we respect the reconciliation efforts it is making, and we wanted an opportunity to tell South Africa and the entire world about our Amy," Linda Biehl explained to me.

Arriving at the hall, they found themselves in a narrow corridor. Suddenly the four murderers appeared before them. Without thinking too much, the Biehls held out their hands. Linda Biehl said that once again she felt nothing. The commission complied with the request of the four men, who were given political amnesty, and released. I asked the Biehls what they thought about the murderers. Linda Biehl says that two of them are detestable thugs, but that the other two were sort of "political soldiers." What they did was a result of the brainwashing done to them, and they had in the meantime realized their error. I asked how she knew that, at which point the conversation took a shocking turn. "Actually, they're really nice guys," says Linda Biehl. "They have nice girlfriends, and tomorrow we're taking all of them out to see the musical `Cats.'"

It was hard to hide my stupefaction. Linda Biehl said they hadn't yet seen the musical, and it was now playing in Cape Town. I asked if we were really talking about two of her daughter's murderers and their girlfriends. Had I really understood her right? She and her husband were taking them out? Linda Biehl chuckled, and said that a lot of people are amazed when they hear that she and her husband employ the two murderers in their foundation. For a second, I thought she was pulling my leg, but Linda Biehl chuckled again and said, "Oh, I thought you knew. Didn't you notice the fellow sitting by the front door next to the computer? That's Ntobeko."

While I was still wondering what to say, Mrs. Biehl called the two fellows over to join us. The other one's name is Easy Nofemela. For the next 45 minutes we sat together - Amy Biehl's two murderers, her two parents and me.

Ntobeko Penni is 28, Nofemela is 30. Both now work at the foundation. "They didn't have any work when they got out of prison, and we needed people," explained Peter Biehl. Penni actually wanted to be a social worker or journalist, but has a good head for business. The foundation is supposedly helping him to start a driving school; that is his dream. Nofemela will receive a bakery, if I understood Peter Biehl correctly. "We thought that would fulfill the reconciliation. This is the meaning of Ubuntu," says Biehl.

I told the two killers that I could not understand Amy's parents, and asked them to explain to me the meaning of the Biehl's attitude toward them. Ntobeko Penni said that he didn't really understand it, either, but that he is grateful to them. He calls Linda Biehl "Makulu" - grandmother in Zulu. Linda Biehl said it was so, and that it makes her happy, because her new grandson Alexander, born in America, also calls her Makulu. Ntobeko said that it was definitely harder for me to understand the relationship since I evidently don't understand what Ubuntu is.

Anthropologists have studied the term, and explained the concept of reconciliation that derives from it. If I correctly understand what I read, when a person harms his fellow man, he also harms the perfection of his own humanity, and thereby also harms the perfection of his victim's humanity. When the victim forgives him, he restores the humanity of the attacker, and in so doing also restores his own humanity. Ntobeko Penni explained that Ubuntu also has meaning in everyday life, the essence of which is mutual aid. If someone has nothing to eat, someone will share his bread with him. If someone has nowhere to sleep, someone will offer him a place in his home.

There are local reconciliation committees on every street, operating as quasi magistrate's courts: If two people are fighting, the neighborhood reconciliation committee sees to it that they settle the argument. Penni said that Ubuntu, which he learned in his childhood from his father, also requires one to show respect and dignity to every person, in other words, a sort of African humanism.

I said that it indeed sounded very noble and asked him, then, why he murdered Amy. "Ahhh," Ntobeko Penni said, "The rules of Ubuntu don't apply to whites." "No, no," said Linda Biehl, "Ubuntu doesn't apply to whites."

Days of Ubuntu - part 3
Where are the graves? Mary Burton believes that much of the truth will come out and that this is certainly important, because, like Archbishop Tutu, she believes there can be no healing without truth. But a significant share of the truth is not being revealed. Burton is referring mainly to the acts committed by heads of the military and political establishments. Many of them just don't give a damn about the commission. The law empowered the commission to subpoena and interrogate witnesses
Mar. 26, 2002 | 12:00 AM

Where are the graves?

Mary Burton believes that much of the truth will come out and that this is certainly important, because, like Archbishop Tutu, she believes there can be no healing without truth. But a significant share of the truth is not being revealed. Burton is referring mainly to the acts committed by heads of the military and political establishments. Many of them just don't give a damn about the commission. The law empowered the commission to subpoena and interrogate witnesses, but too many people avoided the hearings, lied to the commission or simply ignored it. The commission missed many opportunities, says Burton. She blames herself, too. She and the other commission members weren't strong enough.

Cape Town District Court Judge Dennis Davis took me one morning on a climb to the peak of Lions Head, to gaze at the ocean. The view from the mountain top is breathtaking: Cape Town is rightly considered one of the most beautiful cities in the world. As we were climbing, the judge explained that the Truth and Reconciliation Commission's work had convinced him that the criminal law process was better suited to uncovering the truth. The fact is, Davis said, that the chance of getting an amnesty had not convinced the generals and leading statesmen to appear before the committee.

Constitutional Court Justice Albie Sachs disagrees. He unwillingly admits to the limitations of the judicial system, but in his estimate, the Truth and Reconciliation Commission exposed much more information than might have been exposed by the ordinary wheels of justice. No, Sachs confirmed, not all of the criminals had paid their debt, but the commission had achieved a great deal, including the revelation of what befell many of the people who had disappeared, and where they were buried. The commission could not bring the dead back to life, but at least it gave graves to their relatives.

Essentially, it gave them something even more important: an opportunity to tell their story. In that respect, the commission hearings are a little like the Eichmann trial. It too was designed as a sort of national spectacle, the sentence known in advance. But the trial enabled Holocaust survivors to tell a story that until then Israel was not ready to hear, and in those terms it opened up a process of national therapy. The same sort of thing happened in South Africa. That process is still in its infancy.

The Eichmann trial gave birth to an important theory about the nature of the murderer and the "banality of evil." The TRC hearings only partially confirmed this thesis. Many apartheid criminals did what they did out of a firm belief in their cause, after undergoing a gradual process of hardening. Like the Nazis, they did not see the blacks as human beings like themselves, and the more they humiliated and abused them - the more the humanity of their victims was reduced, and the ideology seemed to fulfill itself.

Transcripts of the commission's proceedings can provide an important basis for investigation of the subject, but Mary Burton says the commission did not take the required steps to ensure that its work would be properly archived and available for research purposes. She therefore fears that the hearings will in time be forgotten. But the biggest blunder has to do with compensation, she feels. That is also of greatest concern to Anglican priest Michael Lapsley.

Where is the money?

Lapsley came to South Africa from New Zealand. He did not arrive with political opinions, but influenced by the situation, he joined the ANC. Members of the group ascertained that his life was in danger, and sent him to Zimbabwe. One day he arrived home from a lecture tour in Canada and began opening the letters that had piled up in his absence. Lapsley pulled off the plastic wrapper of an envelope of theology journals, detonating the explosives inside. He remembers flying into the air; he remained fully conscious the entire time. Lapsley lost both hands and one eye. He is now the director of the Institute for Healing of Memories, a sort of workshop that helps victims of the former regime to cope with their traumas. Lapsley offers this caveat: All of the achievements of the TRC could come to naught if the government does not compensate victims of the old regime.

This was one of the commission's main recommendations. About 20,000 persons harmed by the regime are supposed to receive $2,000 each, in six annual payments, totaling approximately $40 million. But the government has not yet paid, and many victims of apartheid feel betrayed. This does not encourage a mood of reconciliation.

And it's not the only thing: South Africa is rife with unemployment, crime and Aids. About 250,000 people are dying of the plague every year; most are black. I asked Minister Kader Asmal about the compensation, and he offered, in a bureaucratic tone of voice, that the commission must first submit its final report, meaning that it must still compose two more volumes of mainly technical material. It is amazing how quickly underground fighters can adopt the excuses of government.

Kader's friends

Asmal invited me to a family celebration. He and his English wife Louise were celebrating their 40th wedding anniversary. For the occasion, they had rented the restaurant in the world-famous Kirstenbosch botanical gardens. A special menu had been printed on gold paper: The appetizers were Mediterranean style, the main course was a chicken curry. The guests, most of whom were Indian or black, came in formal wear. Many drove up in Mercedes sedans. Men with clear plastic buttons in their ears walked about in the parking lot and entrance; before being allowed into the restaurant, the guests were asked to pass through metal detectors. And Nelson Mandela was there, too.

Mandela, 85 and white-haired, radiates nobility and charisma; his presence at the celebration will presumably be considered one of the high points of the minister's career. Half a dozen ministers attended Asmal's celebration, and guests came from abroad as well, anti-apartheid activists from England and Holland. Mandela sat at the head table. Some of the guests dared to approach him; a few were honored with an invitation to sit down with him for a minute or two. He wore an African shirt, and put on an informal mood. But the whole gathering seemed like a party-event. Asmal had met his wife while working for the ANC; everything they'd been through together was related to the national struggle. Their life was the struggle, the struggle was their life.

Once Mandela left, the men loosened their ties, the minister's grandchildren recited humorous rhymes to Grandpa Kader and Grandma Louise, and there was also dancing, to music that was a blend of Western and African beats. I had the impression I was with a new elite celebrating its victory with comfort, not yet with arrogance. This, I imagined, is how Mapai insiders celebrated in the 1950s. And if they were close enough - David Ben-Gurion might even grace their parties. The future was theirs. At Tony Leon's party, on the other hand, a dejected atmosphere prevailed. The guests were bogged down in the past.

Tony's friends

Tony Leon, leader of the opposition, heads a small party that is not unlike Shinui and Meretz. It isn't easy being the opposition to a national unity government; the ANC shares rule with the National Party, which was the party of apartheid. The National Alliance, which Leon heads, recently lost a few parliament deputies, who went over to the government's side.

He is an impressive man who exudes great personal charm. Leon lives in a nice home with an Israeli wife, Michal (nee Even-Zahav), and their two children. Leon, his wife and two children attempted to set a relaxed atmosphere, but the folks gathered round the family swimming pool were in no mood to celebrate. They know, of course, that the white time is passing. I spoke at length with Colin Eglin, a MP who with Helen Suzman and others cried for many years the cry of their beloved country, of liberals and patriots against the injustice of apartheid. I believe I learned a lot from him, there alongside the Leon family pool. The zeal with which the old regime oppressed its enemies infused their struggle with hope. Eglin spoke of those days almost nostalgically.

We spoke about the rewriting of history. Everyone now uses history for political purposes. After the flag and the anthem, they start changing names of entire cities, to adapt them to the whims of the current regime. There is a new museum in Johannesburg that documents the apartheid, which visitors are required to enter in two separate lines, one for whites, one for blacks. A guide beckoned me over to a wall that commemorates over 160 underground fighters sentenced to death during apartheid. One case provides an especially detailed description. It is no coincidence that this one was selected - the judge who handed down the death sentence was Ramon Leon, whom the guide emphatically explains is the father of Tony Leon.

Regrettably, it would be hard to say that the old regime collapsed as the result of activities of the liberal opposition. Many whites now claim that they had always, either openly or not, opposed apartheid. This is also the dominant trend in the new Jewish museum in Cape Town. In a somewhat pathetic attempt to adapt history to the new situation, the museum emphasizes the part played by Jews in opposing discrimination against blacks, as if they represented the entire community. The truth is that some Jews stood out for their role in the struggle against apartheid, but most of them made their peace with the old regime and benefited from the privileges they were granted as whites. The museum does not conceal the fact, but minimizes it as much as possible.

It reminded me of a line in Nadine Gordimer's "July's People": "The present was his; he would arrange the past to suit it." Nadine Gordimer attended several sessions of the Truth and Reconciliation Commission. She can identify with those willing to forgive and reconcile, she told me, but she can also understand those who refuse to reconcile, and demand vengeance. She mostly identified with victims of the regime who refuse to forgive, but do not seek revenge. She thinks this is how she would react if she were in their shoes. She would want not want to have anything to do with the hearings, she said.


Home Print Edition News
S. Africa decries anti-Semitism at Durban racism conference
By Benjamin Pogrund	| Mar. 27, 2002 | 12:00 AM
	
The South African government has condemned the anti-Semitism at the non-governmental conference against racism held in Durban last August. Referring to the "disgraceful events," Deputy Minister for Foreign Affairs Aziz Pahad said the conference was hijacked and turned into an anti-Semitic event.

Pahad made his comments in a speech earlier this month at the annual conference of the South African Zionist Federation in Johannesburg.

Pahad also used the speech to confirm what he said were "immutable pillars" of South African policy: "unequivocal and unchanging support" for Israel's right to exist within defined borders, in peace and security with its neighbors; utter condemnation of terrorism against Israeli civilians; and total support for an independent Palestinian state.

The South African stand carries considerable weight because of the country's post-apartheid moral voice and its leadership role in Africa and the non-aligned movement.

The anti-Semitism in Durban, described by Jewish participants as the most virulent seen in public since the 1930s, shocked Jews throughout the world. It caused grave anxiety among South Africa's own 80,000-strong Jewish community, especially because of the rise of militant Muslim groups in the country.

The South African government kept silent at the time, to the dismay of those who applauded its commitment to fight group hatred. The conference of non-governmental organizations (NGOs) immediately preceded the United Nations World Conference against Racism.

The events of September 11 overtook the Durban conferences and has pushed them into the background. But the anti-racism declaration issued by governments is an enduring message and is being pursued. At the same time, the NGO declaration which labelled Israel an "apartheid state" and Zionism as "racist" is being picked up by some to attack Israel.

Pahad said that he wanted to make it "unequivocally clear" that his government recognized that the NGO part of the conference "was hijacked and used by some with an anti-Israeli agenda to turn it into an anti-Semitic event." It was why the world's governments had refused to take the NGO statement into their final document.

"Additionally, the South African government as chair worked hard to ensure an acceptable and honorable outcome of the final document which avoided singling out Israel for exclusive criticism in regard to the current crisis in the Middle East. So successful were our efforts in this regard, that our president was personally thanked for South Africa's role in ensuring that outcome by Israeli Foreign Minister Shimon Peres," he said.

Home News
Jordanian FA urges FIFA to ban Israel from soccer events
By Reuters	| Apr. 3, 2002 | 12:00 AM

The Jordanian Football Association urged world soccer's governing body to freeze Israel's participation in FIFA events in the same way it had banned once-apartheid South Africa.

The JFA said in a letter obtained Wednesday that its request to FIFA was prompted by Israel's intensified military escalation against the Palestinians and refusal to abide by United Nations resolutions and withdraw from Palestinian territories.

"In order to uphold its banner of 'fair play,' the entire football community should freeze Israel's participation in this noble sport," said JFA President Prince Ali in a two-page letter dated Tuesday.

Ali, who is a half brother of Jordan's King Abdullah II, said Israel should be banished "the same way it (FIFA) did with the former apartheid South Africa."

The ban should remain "until such time when Israel abides by international law, United Nations resolutions and the will of the world and restores the dignity and freedom to the occupied people of Palestine," Ali said in his letter.

Ali is in charge of an elite commando unit supervising the security of the monarch, but he holds no political portfolio under the constitution that bans royal family members from holding public office.

His remarks, however, underline official frustrations with Jordan's policy of peace with neighboring Israel. Amman on Monday threatened to take unspecified measures in its relations with Israel, ratified under a 1994 peace treaty.

Senior government officials have said any decision, such as dismissing Israeli Ambassador David Dadonn, will be carefully studied.

Ali is also head of the West Asian Football Federation, which groups Syria, Lebanon, Iraq, Iran, Jordan and the Palestinian territories.

Knesset approves 3 new ministers
By Gideon Alon	| Apr. 9, 2002 | 12:00 AM

The Knesset plenum yesterday voted in favor of three new ministers joining the government, bringing the number of cabinet ministers to 27.

Fifty-one MKs from the Likud and the right-wing parties cast their votes in support of the two new ministers from the National Religious Party, Effi Eitam and Yitzhak Levy, and of David Levy from Gesher - while 32 MKs from Labor, Meretz, Hadash, Shinui, the Center Party and the Arab factions voted against.
